والقسم الثاني : يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن المتساوى على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وذلك يلزم منه نفي الصانع . وأيضا : فعلى هذا التقدير يكون وقوع الفعل بدلا عن الترك : محض الاتفاق ، ولم يكن مستندا إلى العبد ، فيكون الجبر لازما .
وأما القسم الأول : فنقول : ذلك المرجح . إما أن يكون من العبد ، أومن غيره ، أولا منه ولا من غيره . لا جائز أن يكون من العبد . والا عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، ويلزم إما التسلسل وإما الجبر
والقسم الثالث : فنقول : أيضا : باطل . لأنه يقتضي جواز حدوث الشيء ، لا لمحدث ، ولا لمؤثر . ويلزم منه نفي الصانع . ويلزم منه أيضا : القول الجبر - على ما بيناه - ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت : أن ذلك المرجح ، إنما حدث بإحداث الغير. فنقول : ذلك المرجح إنما يكون مرجحا ، إذا اقتضى رجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، وعند حصول هذا الرجحان يجب الفعل . وذلك لأن طرف الترك ، حال كونه مساويا لطرف الفعل ، كان ممتنع الرجحان . فحال حصوله مرجوحا ، أولى أن يصير ممتنع الرجحان . وإذا
- لها . وإنما الله يخلق في هذا القلم تحركا بعد تحرك . هكذا دائها طالما القلم متحركا . فإذا سكن . لم يكن حتى خلق الله فيه أيضا سكونا ، ولا يبرح يخلق فيه سكونا بعد سكون ، طالما القلم ساكنا ففي كل أن من تلك الآنات - أعني الأزمنة المنفردة - يخلق الله عرضا في جميع أشخاص الموجودات . من ملك وفلك وغيرهما ، هكذا دائما في كل حين . وقالوا : إن هذا هو الإيمان الحقيقي بإن الله فاعل. ومن لم يعتقد أن هكذا يفعل الله ، فقد جحد كون الله فاعلا - على رأيهم : [ دلالة الحائرين ص ٢٠٨ ] .
ذكر هذا النص : موسى بن ميمون ، وعلق عليه بقوله : في مثل هذه الاعتقادات يقال عندي وعند كل ذي عقل : ( أم أنتم تخدعونه، كما يخدع إنسان ، ؟ [ ٩:١٢ ] إذ هذا هو عين
الخدعة حقيقة .
(١) من ( ط ، ل )
المرجوح ممتنعا ، صار الراجح واجبا . ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين فثبت : أن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على أن يفعل غيره فيه
هذا المرجح .
واعلم : أنه متى فعل ذلك الغير فيه ذلك المرجح ، وجب صدور الفعل عنه . فثبت : أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، يوجب الفعل ، وأنه تعالى ، إن خلقهما : وجب الفعل . وإن لم يخلق مجموعهما : امتنع الفعل . فثبت : أن العبد غير مستقل بنفسه في الفعل وفي الترك . وهو مطلوب
As for the first division: We say: Since that power is suitable for action and inaction, the preponderance of one of the two sides over the other either depends on a determinant or does not depend.
The second division: It would entail the preponderance of one of the two equal sides of the possible over the other without a determinant. That necessitates denial of the Maker. Also, on this supposition, the occurrence of action instead of inaction is sheer coincidence, and it would not be attributed to the servant, so compulsion is necessary.
As for the first division: We say: That determinant is either from the servant, or from another, or from neither. It is not possible that it be from the servant, because then the first division would recur in it, leading to either infinite regress or compulsion.
The third division: We say: It is also false, because it would entail the possibility of a thing's coming to existence without a cause and without an effect, leading to denial of the Maker and also the statement of compulsion, as we have explained. Since these two divisions are false, it is established that that determinant only comes to be by the bringing into existence of another. We say: That determinant is only a determinant if it entails the preponderance of the side of action over the side of inaction, and when this preponderance occurs, the action becomes necessary. That is because the side of inaction, while being equal to the side of action, was impossible of preponderance. So when it becomes preponderated, it is even more impossible of preponderance. And if
... her. And God only creates in this pen movement after movement. Thus its da'iha as long as the pen is moving. Then when it stops, it is not until God also creates in it stillness, and He continues to create in it stillness after stillness, as long as the pen is still. So in every instant of those instants—I mean the discrete times—God creates an accident in all individuals of existents, from angel and sphere and others, thus always in every moment. And they said: This is the true belief that God is an agent. And whoever does not believe that God does thus has denied that God is an agent—according to their view: [Guide of the Perplexed p. 208].
This text was mentioned: Moses Maimonides, and he commented on it saying: Regarding such beliefs it is said with me and with every intelligent person: (Or do you deceive him, as one deceives a human? [9:12] Indeed, this is the very essence of deception.
(1) from (T, L)
The preponderated is impossible, so the preponderant becomes necessary. Necessarily because there is no exit from the two opposites. So it is established that the occurrence of the action from the servant is dependent on that another does in him this determinant.
And note: Whenever that other does that determinant in him, the action necessarily issues from him. So it is established that the combination of power with the motive necessitates the action, and that the Exalted One, if He creates both of them, the action becomes necessary; and if He does not create their combination, the action is impossible. So it is established that the servant is not independent by himself in action and inaction. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) aiming to establish that human action is not self-sufficient but depends on a determinant (murajjiḥ) created by another (ultimately God). The author begins by dividing the question of preponderance into two possibilities: either the preponderance of action over inaction depends on a determinant or it does not. The second option (no determinant) is rejected because it would entail the denial of the Maker (since it implies uncaused occurrence) and would lead to compulsion (jabr) as the action would be mere coincidence, not attributable to the servant. Returning to the first option, the nature of the determinant is examined: it must be from the servant, from another, or from neither. The possibility of it being from the servant is invalid because that would restart the same division within the servant's power, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or compulsion. The third possibility (neither from servant nor another) is also false because it would allow a thing to occur without a cause, again denying the Maker and implying compulsion. Thus, the determinant must come from another (i.e., God). The author then defines the determinant: it must arise from the directive (dā‘ī) that makes action necessary. When the side of inaction was equal to action, it was impossible to preponderate; once it becomes preponderated, it is even more impossible. The conclusion (after a quoted interjection on continuous creation from Maimonides) is that when the other (God) creates the determinant in the servant, the action necessarily follows. Hence, the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dā‘ī) necessitates the action; if God creates both, action is necessary; if He does not, action is impossible. Thus the servant is not independent in either acting or refraining—which is the sought conclusion.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjiḥ)
قدرة (qudra)
داعي (dā‘ī)
جبر (jabr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
بديهيات (badīhiyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if action depends on God, then human responsibility is negated.
Correction: The author only shows the servant is not independent in creating his actions, but the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb holds that the servant acquires the action, preserving moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion the author reaches about the servant's ability to act?
A) The servant is completely independent in action and inaction.
B) The servant's action depends on a determinant from another, and the servant is not independent.
C) The servant's action is purely coincidental with no cause.
D) The servant creates his own actions without any determinant.

Key Takeaway
The servant's action necessarily depends on a determinant created by God, so the servant is not independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action—good or bad—do you genuinely attribute its existence to God's creation of the determinant within you, or do you secretly feel your own autonomy deserves the credit? What would change if you lived with constant awareness of this dependence?
?
The proof shows that without God's determinant, your actions would be impossible. How does this truth confront your pride in your achievements or your shame in your failures? Can you name one specific area where you need to surrender the illusion of self-sufficiency?
?
If every movement of your will is dependent on a determinant from God, then your excuses for disobeying Him—'I couldn't help it' or 'I'm weak'—lose their force. What will you do differently today to seek His determinant for obedience?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Critique of Continuous Creation and the Deception of Extreme Occasionalism
Author quotes Maimonides' view of continuous creation and refutes it as deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the Maimonidean view that God creates all accidents at every instant, and why he labels it as deception.

Core Idea
The claim of continuous creation by God at every instant leads to deception and contradicts rational causality.

In this excerpt, the author quotes from Moses Maimonides' 'Guide of the Perplexed' an extreme occasionalist view that God creates movement and rest in a pen at every instant. The author then strongly criticizes this position, calling it deception and implying that it cannot be reconciled with sound reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maimonidean quote
Read the description of continuous creation: God creates movement after movement, and stillness after stillness, at every discrete instant.
This view is attributed to certain theologians who hold that God is the sole agent at every moment.
2
Understand the claim
The quote claims that true belief is to hold that God is the direct creator of all accidents at all times, and that denying this is denial of God as agent.
3
Note the author's response
The author then mentions that Maimonides himself commented on this view, calling it outright deception, comparing it to tricking a human.
The author seems to endorse Maimonides' criticism and uses it to reject the extreme occasionalism.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author might include this criticism: to differentiate his own view of determinant from the excess of occasionalism, preserving a role for secondary causality.

So in every instant of those instants—I mean the discrete times—God creates an accident in all individuals of existents, from angel and sphere and others, thus always in every moment. And they said: This is the true belief that God is an agent. And whoever does not believe that God does thus has denied that God is an agent—according to their view: [Guide of the Perplexed p. 208].
This text was mentioned: Moses Maimonides, and he commented on it saying: Regarding such beliefs it is said with me and with every intelligent person: (Or do you deceive him, as one deceives a human? [9:12] Indeed, this is the very essence of deception.
(1) from (T, L)
The preponderated is impossible, so the preponderant becomes necessary. Necessarily because there is no exit from the two opposites. So it is established that the occurrence of the action from the servant is dependent on that another does in him this determinant.
And note: Whenever that other does that determinant in him, the action necessarily issues from him. So it is established that the combination of power with the motive necessitates the action, and that the Exalted One, if He creates both of them, the action becomes necessary; and if He does not create their combination, the action is impossible. So it is established that the servant is not independent by himself in action and inaction. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) aiming to establish that human action is not self-sufficient but depends on a determinant (murajjiḥ) created by another (ultimately God). The author begins by dividing the question of preponderance into two possibilities: either the preponderance of action over inaction depends on a determinant or it does not. The second option (no determinant) is rejected because it would entail the denial of the Maker (since it implies uncaused occurrence) and would lead to compulsion (jabr) as the action would be mere coincidence, not attributable to the servant. Returning to the first option, the nature of the determinant is examined: it must be from the servant, from another, or from neither. The possibility of it being from the servant is invalid because that would restart the same division within the servant's power, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or compulsion. The third possibility (neither from servant nor another) is also false because it would allow a thing to occur without a cause, again denying the Maker and implying compulsion. Thus, the determinant must come from another (i.e., God). The author then defines the determinant: it must arise from the directive (dā‘ī) that makes action necessary. When the side of inaction was equal to action, it was impossible to preponderate; once it becomes preponderated, it is even more impossible. The conclusion (after a quoted interjection on continuous creation from Maimonides) is that when the other (God) creates the determinant in the servant, the action necessarily follows. Hence, the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dā‘ī) necessitates the action; if God creates both, action is necessary; if He does not, action is impossible. Thus the servant is not independent in either acting or refraining—which is the sought conclusion.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
عرض ('arad)
آنات (ānāt)
جبر (jabr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that to affirm God as the sole agent, one must believe He creates every accident at every instant, as in extreme occasionalism.
Correction: The author, following Maimonides, argues that this view is actually deception, not true belief. The true position is that God creates the determinant, but actions have a real (though dependent) occurrence from the servant.

Checkpoint
What does the author's mention of Maimonides' comment serve to do?
A) Support the extreme occasionalist view.
B) Refute it as deception.
C) Show that Maimonides agrees with the author's view.
D) Prove that God creates all actions directly.

Key Takeaway
Extreme occasionalism, which claims God directly creates every accident at every instant, is criticized as deceptive; the author's view of the determinant offers a more nuanced understanding of divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a small, everyday event—like a pen falling or a delay in your plans—do you immediately think of it as directly created by God, or do you acknowledge the natural means He has put in place? This passage warns that an extreme view of divine action can be a deception. In what area of your life might you be ignoring secondary causes and thereby reducing your sense of personal responsibility?
?
Maimonides calls the continuous creation view 'the very essence of deception.' Have you ever held a belief about God that seemed pious but actually limited your understanding of His wisdom and the order He established? Ask yourself: What am I afraid to admit about the role of natural causation because it might seem to diminish God's power?
?
The extreme occasionalist view makes God directly responsible for every movement of a pen. If you truly believed that, how would it change your attitude toward effort and planning? Would you still strive, or would you become passive? This passage challenges you to find the balance between affirming God's sovereignty and engaging with the world He has made. Which side are you leaning toward, and why?

Objection from Free Will and the Answer by Turning to the Unseen
Objection from self-evident free will and answer by applying proof to the Unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his causal proof against the objection that it contradicts self-evident free will, and how he turns the argument to the Unseen.

Core Idea
The objection from free will is answered by applying the same principle to God.

The author now faces a powerful objection: if power plus motive necessitates action, then human free will is an illusion. But we experience freedom directly. How can the theologian respond? In this passage, the author first acknowledges the force of the objection, then shows that the same reasoning applies to God's action, thus turning the tables.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines where the objector claims the proof contradicts self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt). Note the claim that we find in ourselves a necessary, undeniable experience of free choice.
The objector uses 'we find in ourselves necessarily' to ground his objection in immediate experience.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector argues that if power + motive = necessary action, then the servant has no status (makāna) or ability (makna) to choose. But we experience the ability to act or refrain, so the proof must be false.
3
See the author's concession
The author says 'We concede...' but then redirects. He does not deny the experience; instead, he shows the same proof applies to the Unseen (God). This is a classic kalam move: applying the argument to the divine case.
Notice the phrase 'muṭṭarid bi ‘aynihi fī al-ghā’ib' — the proof is exactly applicable.
4
Follow the dilemma for God
The author presents a dilemma: either the determinants for God's creation were present in eternity or not. If yes, then God would be a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not a free agent (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār). If no, the discussion continues.
This shows that the objector's own logic would force an unwanted conclusion about God unless they accept the author's proof.
5
Recognize the argumentative strategy
The author uses the objector's premise (that the proof leads to necessitation) to show that it applies equally to God, forcing the objector to either reject the premise consistently or accept the proof.
This is a dialectical turn: the author does not refute the experience but shows the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الاستدلال في مقابلة البديهيات ، فيكون مردودا . بيان أنه في مقابلة البديهيات : إن على هذا التقدير ، متى حصل مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، كان الفعل واجب الوقوع ، ولم يكن للعبد مكانة واختيار في الفعل . فعلى هذا التقدير ، يكون حال العبد دائرا بين أن يجب صدور الفعل عنه ، وبين أن يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه . وعلى هذا التقدير : يلزم أن لا يكون للعبد مكنة واختيار في الفعل والترك. وذلك باطل في بدائه العقول أنفسنا وجدانا ضروريا أنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا ، وإن شئنا الترك تركنا . فثبت : أن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل : واقع في مقابلة البديهيات . فوجب أن لا يستحق الجواب
لأننا نجد من
سلمنا : أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل غير واقع في مقابلة البديهيات ؛ إلا أننا نقول : هذا الدليل الذي ذكرتم ، مطرد بعينه في الغائب . وذلك لأن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرا في العالم. إما أن يقال : إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل ، أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل . فإن كان الاول ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع الأمور التي لأجلها كان الباري مرجحا لوجود العالم على عدمه، كان حاصلا في الأزل . وأنتم زعمتم : أنه متى حصل الرجحان ، فقد حصل الوجوب . وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم كونه تعالى : موجبا بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار . وإن كان الثاني : نقلنا الكلام في
If it is said: This reasoning opposes self-evident truths, so it is rejected. The explanation that it opposes self-evident truths: On this supposition, whenever the combination of power and motive occurs, the action is necessarily occurring, and the servant has no status and choice in the action. On this supposition, the state of the servant oscillates between the necessity of the action's occurrence from him and the impossibility of the action's occurrence from him. And on this supposition, it entails that the servant has no ability and choice in action and inaction. And that is false according to the self-evident beginnings of our intellects, for we find in ourselves necessarily that if we will the action we do it, and if we will the inaction we do it. So it is established that what you mentioned of the proof is opposed to self-evident truths. So it does not deserve a reply.
We concede: that what you mentioned of the proof is not opposed to self-evident truths; but we say: This proof you mentioned is applicable exactly to the Unseen. That is because everything necessary for His being effective in the world is either said to have been present in eternity, or not present in eternity. If it is the first, then it would entail that the combination of matters for which the Creator was a determinant for the existence of the world over its non-existence was present in eternity. And you claimed that whenever preponderance occurs, necessity occurs. On this supposition, it necessitates Him being necessarily existent by essence, not an agent by choice. And if it is the second, we transfer the discussion into
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the previous proof (that the combination of power and motive necessitates action) contradicts self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt), because we experience immediate, undeniable free will: we find that we can choose to act or refrain. Therefore, the proof should be rejected without further reply. The author counters first rhetorically ('We concede') then substantively: the same reasoning can be applied to the Unseen (ghā’ib), i.e., to God's relation to the world. The objection implicitly assumes that the proof only applies to human acts, but the author shows that it is 'applicable exactly' (muṭṭarid bi ‘aynihi) to God. He then draws a dilemma: either the determinants for God's creation of the world were present in eternity or not. If they were present in eternity, then (by the same logic of the proof) God would be a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt) rather than a free agent (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār), which the objector would likely deny. If they were not present in eternity, then the discussion transfers (nuqilnā al-kalām) — likely continuing the argument, though the text cuts off. Thus the author defends his proof by showing that the objection's own premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion about God, thereby vindicating the original proof.

The Necessity of Action with a Preponderant
Proof that power+motive necessitates action, servant dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the servant's action is necessitated when both power and motive are present, and that this shows the servant's complete dependence on God.

Core Idea
Action is necessitated by power and motive; the servant is never independent.

This lesson unpacks a concise logical proof from the text that establishes a key principle of Ash'ari causality: the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i) makes action necessary. The argument builds on the impossibility of a middle ground between existence and non-existence, and leads to the conclusion that the servant is not the independent originator of his actions. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Note the technical terms: المرجوح, الراجح, المرجح, القدرة, الداعي.
2
Understand the logical premise
The first sentence states that if the weaker side is impossible, the stronger becomes necessary. This is based on the principle of excluded middle: no third option between two opposites.
This is a fundamental logical axiom.
3
Apply to the servant's action
The text then applies this to human action: the action's occurrence depends on a preponderant (murajjih) caused by another (God). When God creates that preponderant, the action necessarily follows.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Combining power and motive necessitates action. If God creates both, action is necessary; if not, action is impossible. Therefore, the servant is not independent in acting or refraining.
This does not negate human choice but situates it within divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرجوح ممتنعا، صار الراجح واجبا. ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين. فثبت: أن صدور الفعل عن العبد، موقوف على أن يفعل غيره فيه هذا المرجح. واعلم: أنه متى فعل ذلك الغير فيه ذلك المرجح، وجب صدور الفعل عنه. فثبت: أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، يوجب الفعل، وأنه تعالى، إن خلقهما: وجب الفعل. وإن لم يخلق مجموعهما: امتنع الفعل. فثبت: أن العبد غير مستقل بنفسه في الفعل وفي الترك. وهو مطلوب.
If the weaker side is impossible, then the stronger becomes necessary. Necessarily, because there is no exit from the two opposites. So it is established: that the occurrence of the action from the servant is dependent on another causing this preponderant in him. And know: whenever that other causes that preponderant in him, the occurrence of the action becomes necessary. So it is established: that the sum of power (qudra) with the motive (da'i) necessitates the action, and that He (Exalted), if He creates both, the action becomes necessary; and if He does not create their sum, the action becomes impossible. So it is established: that the servant is not independent by himself in action and in refraining. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the necessity of a preponderant (murajjih) for any action, leading to the conclusion that the servant (al-'abd) is not independent in his actions. The argument begins by stating that if the weaker side (al-marjuh) is impossible, then the stronger side (al-rajih) becomes necessary. This is because there is no third possibility (no exit from the two opposites). The author then applies this to human action: the occurrence of an action from the servant depends on something else causing a preponderant in him. When that other (i.e., God) creates that preponderant, the action necessarily occurs. Thus, the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i) necessitates the action. If God creates both, the action is necessary; if He does not create their sum, the action is impossible. Therefore, the servant is not independent in acting or refraining, which is the desired conclusion of the proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies human choice entirely and makes humans like machines.
Correction: The proof does not deny human choice; it shows that choice depends on God's creation of the preponderant (murajjih). The servant still has power and motive, but their combination is not self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this truth shift your repentance from mere regret to a plea for God to create the motive for obedience?
?
If your power and motive are created by God, then your will is not self-sufficient. When you pray for strength to do good, do you truly believe that only God can tip the scales? Or do you rely on your own resolve? Examine the last time you succeeded in a good deed—did you attribute it to your own effort or to God's creation of the preponderant?

The text says: 'if He creates both, the action becomes necessary; if He does not, it becomes impossible.' Does this make you feel passive or accountable? Objection: The Proof Contradicts Self-Evident Free Will
Objection that the proof contradicts self-evident free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that the proof of causality negates human free will and thus contradicts self-evident truths.

Core Idea
Self-evident free will challenges any proof that makes human action necessary.

The objector raises a powerful objection: our direct experience of free will seems to contradict any reasoning that makes human action necessary. Before we can accept the previous proof, we must address this challenge. In this lesson, we examine the objector's claim and its basis in self-evident experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic text from 'فإن قيل' to 'فوجب أن لا يستحق الجواب'. Then read the translation. Notice that the objector first states the conclusion, then explains why.
The objector uses the phrase 'وجدانا ضروريا' (necessary finding) to emphasize the self-evidence of free will.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
The objector says the proof 'contradicts self-evident truths'. Identify the specific reasoning: whenever power and motive combine, action becomes necessary, eliminating human choice.
3
Understand the experiential evidence
The objector appeals to inner experience: 'we find in ourselves necessarily that if we will the action, we do it, and if we will refraining, we refrain.' Reflect on why this is considered self-evident.
This is a key argument against deterministic causality in human actions.
4
Consider the objector's conclusion
The objector concludes that the proof does not deserve a response because it contradicts self-evident truths. Ask yourself: does this objection successfully refute the proof?
The answer lesson will respond to this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا الاستدلال في مقابلة البديهيات، فيكون مردودا. بيان أنه في مقابلة البديهيات: إن على هذا التقدير، متى حصل مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، كان الفعل واجب الوقوع، ولم يكن للعبد مكانة واختيار في الفعل. فعلى هذا التقدير، يكون حال العبد دائرا بين أن يجب صدور الفعل عنه، وبين أن يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه. وعلى هذا التقدير: يلزم أن لا يكون للعبد مكنة واختيار في الفعل والترك. وذلك باطل في بدائه العقول؛ لأننا نجد من أنفسنا وجدانا ضروريا أنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا، وإن شئنا الترك تركنا. فثبت: أن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل: واقع في مقابلة البديهيات. فوجب أن لا يستحق الجواب. سلمنا: أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل غير واقع في مقابلة البديهيات؛ إلا أننا نقول: هذا الدليل الذي ذكرتم، مطرد بعينه في الغائب. وذلك لأن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرا في العالم. إما أن يقال: إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل، أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. فإن كان الأول، فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال: إن مجموع الأمور التي لأجلها كان الباري مرجحا لوجود العالم على عدمه، كان حاصلا في الأزل. وأنتم زعمتم: أنه متى حصل الرجحان، فقد حصل الوجوب. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم كونه تعالى: موجبا بالذات، لا فاعلا بالاختيار. وإن كان الثاني: نقلنا الكلام في حدوث تلك الأمور، ولا يتسلسل. بل ينتهي إلى الصفات الأزلية. وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور، وهو كونه تعالى موجبا بالذات. ثم نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: القادر على الفعل وعلى الترك: يمكنه أن يرجح جانب الفعل، على جانب الترك. وبالعكس من غير مرجح. ألا ترى: أن الهارب من السبع، إذا ظهر له طريقان متساويان. فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر، لا لمرجح. والعطشان إذا خير بين قدحين متساويين فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر، لا لمرجح. والجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين. فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني، لا لمرجح. فثبت: أن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد الطرفين على الآخر لا لمرجح.
If it is said: This reasoning contradicts self-evident truths (badihiyyat), so it is rejected. The explanation that it contradicts self-evident truths: indeed, on this assumption, whenever the sum of power with the motive occurs, the action is necessary of occurrence, and the servant has no station and choice in the action. So on this assumption, the state of the servant alternates between that the occurrence of the action from him is necessary and that it is impossible. And on this assumption, it follows that the servant has no ability and choice in action and refraining. And that is false according to the self-evident truths of intellects; because we find in ourselves necessarily that if we will the action, we do it, and if we will refraining, we refrain. So it is established that the proof you have mentioned contradicts self-evident truths. Therefore, it does not deserve a response. We concede that the proof you have mentioned does not contradict self-evident truths; however, we say: this proof you have mentioned applies exactly to the absent (al-gha'ib). That is because everything necessary for Him (Exalted) to be an agent of influence in the world must be either: it was present in eternity, or it was not present in eternity. If the first, then it follows that the sum of matters for which the Creator was a preponderator for the existence of the world over its non-existence was present in eternity. And you have claimed that whenever preponderance occurs, necessity occurs. On this assumption, it follows that He (Exalted) is a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). And if the second, we transfer the discussion to the occurrence of those matters, and there is no infinite regress; it ends with eternal attributes. Then the mentioned implication returns, which is that He (Exalted) is a necessitator by essence. Then we say: why is it not permissible to say: the one capable of action and refraining can prefer the side of action over the side of refraining, and vice versa, without a preponderant (murajjih)? Do you not see that the one fleeing from a lion, if two equal paths appear to him, he chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant? And the thirsty person, if presented with two equal cups, chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant. And the hungry person, if presented with eating two loaves, chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant. So it is established that the capable one can prefer one of the two sides over the other without a preponderant.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the previous proof contradicts self-evident truths (badihiyyat) because we directly experience our own free will: we find that if we will to act, we act, and if we will to refrain, we refrain. Therefore, the proof is invalid and does not deserve a response. The author (or the respondent) first concedes that the proof does not contradict self-evident truths, but then turns the argument against the objector by applying it to the absent (al-gha'ib), i.e., God. The reasoning is that if the objector's principle (that sum of power and motive necessitates action) is applied to God, it leads to the conclusion that God is a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free agent, which the objector would presumably reject. Furthermore, the respondent provides examples of human choice in indifferent situations (fleeing from a lion, choosing between equal cups, etc.) to argue that a capable agent can prefer one side without a preponderant, thereby rejecting the necessity of a preponderant for choice.

البديهيات
القادر
المرجح
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector denies causality altogether.
Correction: The objector does not deny causality but argues that the specific proof eliminates the experienced reality of human free will.

Checkpoint

A) The existence of God
B) Self-evident truths (badihiyyat)
C) The possibility of miracles
D) The concept of causality

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that the proof negates the self-evident experience of free will, making it invalid.

Answer: Applying the Logic to God and Denying the Need for a Preponderant
Answer to the objection, applying logic to God and denying need for preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's twofold response: (1) applying the same reasoning to God leads to necessitarianism, and (2) human choice can occur without a preponderant, as shown by everyday examples.

Core Idea
The objector's own logic, when applied to God, leads to an unwanted conclusion, and choice can occur without a preponderant.

The response to the objection is clever: first, it concedes the point about the proof not contradicting self-evident truths (a rhetorical move). Then it turns the tables by applying the objector's own principle to God, showing that it would force God to be a necessitator rather than a free agent. Additionally, the answer provides everyday examples where humans choose without a preponderant, arguing that a capable agent can simply prefer one option. This lesson unpacks these two prongs of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic text from 'سلمنا' to the end. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a concession, then an application to God, then examples of choice without a preponderant.
The answer does not directly defend the original proof but rather challenges the objector's consistency.
2
Understand the concession and application to God
The answer concedes that the proof does not contradict self-evident truths. Then it says the same proof applies to the 'absent' (al-gha'ib), i.e., God. The reasoning: if the objector's principle (power+motive=necessity) is applied to God, it leads to Him being a necessitator by essence, not a free agent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the objector's own principle leads to a conclusion they would reject.
3
Analyze the argument about preponderance
The answer then argues that a capable agent can prefer one side without a preponderant. It gives three examples: the fleeing person choosing between equal paths, the thirsty choosing between equal cups, and the hungry choosing between two loaves.
These examples are meant to show that choice can occur without a preponderant reason.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The answer concludes that the capable one can prefer one side over the other without a preponderant. This rejects the need for a 'murajjih' in cases of equal alternatives.
This is a direct rebuttal to the objector's implied principle that a preponderant is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا الاستدلال في مقابلة البديهيات، فيكون مردودا. بيان أنه في مقابلة البديهيات: إن على هذا التقدير، متى حصل مجموع القدرة مع الداعي، كان الفعل واجب الوقوع، ولم يكن للعبد مكانة واختيار في الفعل. فعلى هذا التقدير، يكون حال العبد دائرا بين أن يجب صدور الفعل عنه، وبين أن يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه. وعلى هذا التقدير: يلزم أن لا يكون للعبد مكنة واختيار في الفعل والترك. وذلك باطل في بدائه العقول؛ لأننا نجد من أنفسنا وجدانا ضروريا أنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا، وإن شئنا الترك تركنا. فثبت: أن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل: واقع في مقابلة البديهيات. فوجب أن لا يستحق الجواب. سلمنا: أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل غير واقع في مقابلة البديهيات؛ إلا أننا نقول: هذا الدليل الذي ذكرتم، مطرد بعينه في الغائب. وذلك لأن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرا في العالم. إما أن يقال: إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل، أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. فإن كان الأول، فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال: إن مجموع الأمور التي لأجلها كان الباري مرجحا لوجود العالم على عدمه، كان حاصلا في الأزل. وأنتم زعمتم: أنه متى حصل الرجحان، فقد حصل الوجوب. وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم كونه تعالى: موجبا بالذات، لا فاعلا بالاختيار. وإن كان الثاني: نقلنا الكلام في حدوث تلك الأمور، ولا يتسلسل. بل ينتهي إلى الصفات الأزلية. وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور، وهو كونه تعالى موجبا بالذات. ثم نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: القادر على الفعل وعلى الترك: يمكنه أن يرجح جانب الفعل، على جانب الترك. وبالعكس من غير مرجح. ألا ترى: أن الهارب من السبع، إذا ظهر له طريقان متساويان. فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر، لا لمرجح. والعطشان إذا خير بين قدحين متساويين فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر، لا لمرجح. والجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين. فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني، لا لمرجح. فثبت: أن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد الطرفين على الآخر لا لمرجح.
If it is said: This reasoning contradicts self-evident truths (badihiyyat), so it is rejected. The explanation that it contradicts self-evident truths: indeed, on this assumption, whenever the sum of power with the motive occurs, the action is necessary of occurrence, and the servant has no station and choice in the action. So on this assumption, the state of the servant alternates between that the occurrence of the action from him is necessary and that it is impossible. And on this assumption, it follows that the servant has no ability and choice in action and refraining. And that is false according to the self-evident truths of intellects; because we find in ourselves necessarily that if we will the action, we do it, and if we will refraining, we refrain. So it is established that the proof you have mentioned contradicts self-evident truths. Therefore, it does not deserve a response. We concede that the proof you have mentioned does not contradict self-evident truths; however, we say: this proof you have mentioned applies exactly to the absent (al-gha'ib). That is because everything necessary for Him (Exalted) to be an agent of influence in the world must be either: it was present in eternity, or it was not present in eternity. If the first, then it follows that the sum of matters for which the Creator was a preponderator for the existence of the world over its non-existence was present in eternity. And you have claimed that whenever preponderance occurs, necessity occurs. On this assumption, it follows that He (Exalted) is a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). And if the second, we transfer the discussion to the occurrence of those matters, and there is no infinite regress; it ends with eternal attributes. Then the mentioned implication returns, which is that He (Exalted) is a necessitator by essence. Then we say: why is it not permissible to say: the one capable of action and refraining can prefer the side of action over the side of refraining, and vice versa, without a preponderant (murajjih)? Do you not see that the one fleeing from a lion, if two equal paths appear to him, he chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant? And the thirsty person, if presented with two equal cups, chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant. And the hungry person, if presented with eating two loaves, chooses one over the other, not for a preponderant. So it is established that the capable one can prefer one of the two sides over the other without a preponderant.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the previous proof contradicts self-evident truths (badihiyyat) because we directly experience our own free will: we find that if we will to act, we act, and if we will to refrain, we refrain. Therefore, the proof is invalid and does not deserve a response. The author (or the respondent) first concedes that the proof does not contradict self-evident truths, but then turns the argument against the objector by applying it to the absent (al-gha'ib), i.e., God. The reasoning is that if the objector's principle (that sum of power and motive necessitates action) is applied to God, it leads to the conclusion that God is a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free agent, which the objector would presumably reject. Furthermore, the respondent provides examples of human choice in indifferent situations (fleeing from a lion, choosing between equal cups, etc.) to argue that a capable agent can prefer one side without a preponderant, thereby rejecting the necessity of a preponderant for choice.

موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار

Misconception: The answer denies human free will.
Correction: The answer actually defends human free will by arguing that choice can occur without a preponderant, and it uses that to challenge the objector's own reasoning about God.

The Motive Does Not Necessitate: Distinguishing Free Agency
Objection that motive only makes action preferable not necessary, distinguishing free agent from necessitator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a motive (da'i) makes an action more likely but not necessary, and how this distinction preserves free agency against necessitarianism.

Core Idea
A motive tips the balance without forcing the action.

In this passage, the author tackles a second objection: if God creates the motive, why doesn't it compel action? The answer clarifies that a motive makes an action preferable (awla) without reaching necessity, thereby upholding the agent's freedom. This distinction is vital for understanding Islamic theology of voluntary action and divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concession, the objection, and the detailed explanation.
Pay attention to the conditional clause about 'not reaching the level of necessity'.
2
Identify the objection and its premise
Find where the objector concedes that the motive is created by God, then questions why the motive necessitates the action rather than just making it more likely.
The objector is not denying God's role but questioning the inference from motive to necessity.
3
Understand the explanation using equal opposites
The author explains that a capable agent can produce either of two opposites. Without a motive, neither occurs; with a motive, one becomes preferable but not necessary. See how this avoids both coercion and uncaused preponderance.
Key terms: الاستواء (equality), الرجحان (preponderance), الأولى (preferable).
4
Grasp the distinction between free agent and necessitator
The passage concludes that because the motive does not necessitate, coercion (jabr) is avoided, and we can distinguish between a necessitating cause (al-mujib bi al-dhat) and a free agent (al-fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). Reflect on this distinction.
This distinction is central to the Ash'ari view of free will under divine creation of acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا: أنه لا بد في الفعل من الداعي، وأن حصول تلك الداعية بخلق الله تعالى. لكن لم قلتم: إن عند حصول تلك الداعية: يجب الفعل؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن عند حصول تلك الداعية، يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع، مع أنه لا تنتهي تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب؟ بيانه: وهو القادر لما كانت نسبة قادريته إلى الضدين على السوية، امتنع صدور أحدهما بدلا عن الثاني. لأن الاستواء يناقض الرجحان. أما إذا دعاه الداعي إلى فعل أحد الضدين، صار ذلك الضد أولى بالوقوع. لكن بشرط أن لا تنتهي تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب. فلأجل أنه حصل الرجحان، بسبب حصول تلك الداعية، لا يلزم رجحان أحد الطرفين المتساويين على الآخر، لا لمرجح. ولأجل أن تلك الأولوية، لما انتهت إلى حد الوجوب، لم يلزم الجبر وحصل الامتياز بين المؤثر الموجب بالذات، وبين الفاعل بالاختيار. أما قوله: إنه حال الاستواء، لما كان ممتنع الوقوع، كان حال المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع، وإذا امتنع المرجوح، وجب الراجح ضرورة: أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين.
We concede that the action requires a motive (da'i), and that obtaining that motive is by the creation of God the Exalted. But why do you say that when that motive is obtained, the action becomes necessary? And why is it not permissible to say that when that motive is obtained, the action becomes more likely (awla) to occur, while that likelihood does not reach the level of necessity? Its explanation: the capable one, since the ratio of his capability to the two opposites is equal, it is impossible for one to occur instead of the other, because equality contradicts preponderance. But when the motive invites him to one of the two opposites, that opposite becomes more likely to occur, but on condition that that likelihood does not reach the level of necessity. So because preponderance has occurred due to the occurrence of the motive, it does not necessitate a preponderance of one of the two equal sides over the other without a preponderant. And because that likelihood did not reach the level of necessity, coercion (jabr) is not necessitated, and a distinction is made between the necessitating cause by essence (al-mu'aththir al-mujib bi al-dhat) and the free agent (al-fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). As for his statement: 'When in the state of equality it was impossible to occur, then the state of being the weaker side (marjuhiyya) is more deserving of impossibility, and if the weaker side is impossible, then the stronger is necessary, necessarily because there is no exit from the two opposites.'
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its clarification. The objector concedes that a motive is necessary and is created by God, but questions why the motive necessitates the action rather than merely making it more likely (awla). The objector argues that the capable agent has equal power over two opposites, so without a preponderant, neither can occur. However, when the motive invites (da'a) to one opposite, that opposite becomes more likely, but not to the point of necessity. This preserves the distinction between a necessitating cause (which brings about necessity) and a free agent (who acts by choice). The final sentence is a quote from the opponent's previous argument about impossibility of equality leading to necessity; the text likely intends to refute or clarify it, but the passage ends there. The key point is that the motive does not necessitate but merely preponderates, maintaining free choice.

الداعي
الأولى
الوجوب
الاستواء
المرجوح
الموجب بالذات
الفاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies God's role in creating the motive.
Correction: The passage explicitly concedes that the motive is created by God. It only denies that the motive necessitates the action, thus preserving human responsibility while affirming divine causality.

Checkpoint

A) Makes it necessary
B) Makes it possible
C) Makes it more likely without necessity
D) Has no effect

Key Takeaway
A motive makes an action preferable but not necessary, distinguishing free choice from coercion.

Yet you chose obedience. How does that experience confirm the lesson's claim? What does it tell you about your own responsibility?
?
If you believe God creates your motives, do you struggle with feeling that your actions are not truly yours? The lesson insists you are still a free agent. Motive and Preference: Refuting Necessity
The author concedes the need for a motive but denies it necessitates the act; he also refutes the equality objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that a divine motive makes an act preferable but not necessary, and how he refutes the opponent's equality objection.

Core Idea
A motive tips the scale but does not force the hand.

The author engages with a key objection in the debate on divine causation. While conceding that a motive is necessary for action, he insists that the motive does not compel the act; it merely makes it preferable. This distinction preserves free choice and refutes determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Note the argument's flow: concession, explanation, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that the author separates 'preference' (awlawiyya) from 'necessity' (wujūb). The motive makes an act more preferable but not obligatory.
This is the central point of the lesson.
3
Follow the explanation of preference
The author explains that the agent's power relates equally to both opposites. A motive tips the balance, creating preponderance without compulsion.
4
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that if equality makes both actions impossible, then preferring one makes it necessary. Understand the logic of this objection.
5
Understand the refutation
The author replies that the act's original non-existence is eternal and does not require a determinant. The motive makes existence more appropriate, but non-existence remains possible.
This refutation relies on the concept of 'continuous non-existence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أنه لا بد في الفعل من الداعي ، وأن حصول تلك الداعية بخلق الله تعالى . لكن لم قلتم : إن عند حصول تلك الداعية : يجب الفعل ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن عند حصول تلك الداعية ، يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع ، مع أنه لا تنتهي تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب ؟ بيانه : وهو القادر لما كانت نسبة قادريته إلى الضدين على السوية ، امتنع صدور أحدهما بدلا عن الثاني . لأن الاستواء يناقض الرجحان . أما إذا دعاه الداعي إلى فعل أحد الضدين، صار ذلك الضد أولى بالوقوع. لكن بشرط أن لا تنتهي تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب . فلأجل أنه حصل الرجحان ، بسبب حصول تلك الداعية ، لا يلزم رجحان أحد الطرفين المتساويين على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . ولأجل أن تلك الأولوية ، لما انتهت إلى حد الوجوب ، لم يلزم الجبر وحصل الامتياز بين المؤثر الموجب بالذات، وبين الفاعل بالاختيار . أما قوله : إنه حال الاستواء ، لما كان ممتنع الوقوع ، كان حال المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع وإذا امتنع المرجوح ، وجب الراجح ضرورة : أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين فنقول : هذا مغالطة. وذلك لأن الفعل كان معدوما . وعدمه كان مستمرا من الأزل إلى الأبد . والعدم المستمر لا يحتاج إلى المقتضى . فنقول : عند حصول الداعية المرجحة لجانب الوجود . الأولى دخوله في الوجود . وإن كان لا يمتنع بقاؤه على ذلك العدم الأصلي المستمر . ولا نقول : إن هذه القدرة إنما تترجح بسبب الداعية المرجوجة ، وهي داعية الترك . لأن بقاء المعدوم على عدمه الأصلي ، لا يحتاج إلى مرجح ، بل يكون استمراره على ما كان عليه ، لنفسه . وعلى هذا التقدير، فقد زال المحال الذي ذكرتموه .
We concede that there must be a motive for the act and that the occurrence of that motive is by the creation of Allah. But why do you say that when that motive occurs, the act becomes necessary? And why is it not permissible to say that when that motive occurs, the act becomes more preferable to occur, while that preference does not reach the limit of necessity? Its explanation: For the capable agent, since his ability relates equally to two opposites, the occurrence of one instead of the other is impossible because equality contradicts preponderance. But if a motive calls him to one of the two opposites, that opposite becomes more preferable to occur, but on condition that that preference does not reach the limit of necessity. So because preponderance occurs due to the motive, it does not necessitate preponderance of one of two equal sides without a preponderator. And because that preference, when it reaches the limit of necessity, compulsion does not follow, and the distinction between the inherently necessitating agent and the agent by choice is achieved. As for his statement: 'Since at the state of equality occurrence is impossible, the state of being less preferable is more impossible, and if the less preferable is impossible, the preferable must occur by necessity, because there is no escape from the two contradictories.' So we say: This is a fallacy. That is because the act was non-existent, and its non-existence continued from eternity to eternity. Continuous non-existence does not need a determinant. So we say: When the motive that gives preponderance to the side of existence occurs, it is more appropriate for it to come into existence, although it is not impossible for it to remain upon that original continuous non-existence. And we do not say that this ability is preponderated due to the motive of the less preferable, which is the motive of omission, because the continuation of the non-existent upon its original non-existence does not need a preponderator; rather, its continuation as it was is by itself. On this estimation, the impossibility you mentioned has been removed.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a concession followed by a refutation. The author concedes that a motive (dā'ī) is necessary for an act and that the motive is created by Allah. However, he objects to the claim that the motive necessitates the act. Instead, the motive makes the act more preferable (awlawiyya) without reaching the level of necessity (wujūb). He explains that the agent's power is equally related to both opposites, so without a motive, no action would occur. The motive tips the balance, but does not compel. Then he quotes an opponent's objection: if at perfect equality both actions are impossible, then preferring one over the other is even more impossible, so the preferable becomes necessary. The author replies that this is a fallacy because the act was originally non-existent, and its non-existence is eternal and continuous. Eternal non-existence does not require a determinant (muqtaḍā). The motive makes existence more appropriate, but it is not impossible for the act to remain non-existent. The motive for omission does not need a preponderator because non-existence is the original state. Thus, the claimed impossibility is removed.

Key Terms
داع
أولوية
وجوب
ضد
ترجیح
مقتضى
إيجاب
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceding that a motive is necessary for action implies that God's actions are determined, leaving no room for free choice.
Correction: The author distinguishes between preference (awlawiyya) and necessity (wujūb). The motive makes an act preferable, but does not compel it. The act could remain non-existent, preserving contingency and free choice.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the occurrence of a motive not necessitate the act?
A) Because the motive is not created by Allah.
B) Because the act continues to be non-existent eternally.
C) Because the agent can choose otherwise.
D) Because the preference does not reach the limit of necessity.

Key Takeaway
A motive creates preponderance (tarjīḥ) without compulsion, preserving the agent's choice and refuting determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
هل تستخدم فكرة 'الأولوية دون الوجوب' لتبرير تأجيل واجب تشعر بثقله، مع أنك تعلم أن الداعي موجود لكنك تخاف من الالتزام؟ كيف ستحاسب نفسك إذا استمر العدم الأصلي بلا سبب؟
?
عندما تظن أن فعلاً معيناً يجب أن يحدث لمجرد أن لديك الدافع إليه، ألم تكن تتصرف كأنك مجبر؟ أين كان اختيارك الحقيقي في تلك اللحظة؟
?
إذا كان بقاء العدم الأصلي لا يحتاج إلى سبب، فما عذرك في ترك الطاعات مع أن الدواعي كثيرة؟ ألست تدرك أنك تبقى على العدم باختيارك، لا بغير سبب؟

Refuting the Necessity of a Preponderating Cause for Existence
The author refutes the claim that a preponderating factor necessitates existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why continuous non-existence does not require a cause and how the author refutes the claim that a preponderating factor is necessary for an act to exist.

Core Idea
Continuous non-existence needs no cause; only change requires a preponderator.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection in kalam about causality. Some argue that for a thing to come into existence, a preponderating factor must be present. But what about remaining in non-existence? The author explains that continuous non-existence is self-sustaining and does not need a cause, thereby dissolving the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Pay attention to the author's direct refutation tone.
Notice the term 'sophism' (مغالطة) at the start.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between continuous non-existence and the transition to existence. Continuous non-existence does not need a cause, while the transition may involve a preponderating motive.
This distinction is central to the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation logic
The author argues that because non-existence is continuous from eternity, its continuation does not require a preponderator. Only when the preponderating motive for existence arises does the act become appropriate for existence.
4
Apply the reasoning
Explain in your own words why the opposing claim is a sophism. Use the author's point about self-sustaining non-existence.
You can test your understanding with the checkpoint question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : هذا مغالطة. وذلك لأن الفعل كان معدوما . وعدمه كان مستمرا من الأزل إلى الأبد . والعدم المستمر لا يحتاج إلى المقتضى . فنقول : عند حصول الداعية المرجحة لجانب الوجود . الأولى دخوله في الوجود . وإن كان لا يمتنع بقاؤه على ذلك العدم الأصلي المستمر . ولا نقول : إن هذه القدرة إنما تترجح بسبب الداعية المرجوجة ، وهي داعية الترك . لأن بقاء المعدوم على عدمه الأصلي ، لا يحتاج إلى مرجح ، بل يكون استمراره على ما كان عليه ، لنفسه . وعلى هذا التقدير، فقد زال المحال الذي ذكرتموه .
We say: This is a sophism. That is because the act was non-existent. And its non-existence was continuous from eternity to eternity. And continuous non-existence does not need a requisite. We say: When the preponderating motive for the side of existence arises, it is more appropriate for it to enter into existence. Even if it is not impossible for it to remain upon that original continuous non-existence. And we do not say that this capacity is preponderated only by a preponderating motive, and that is the motive of omission. Because the remaining of the non-existent upon its original non-existence does not need a preponderator; rather its continuation upon what it was is by itself. And upon this estimation, the impossibility you mentioned has been removed.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The author addresses a claim that the preponderating factor (الداعية المرجحة) is necessary for the act to exist. He calls it a sophism (مغالطة). The argument: The act was non-existent, and its non-existence was continuous from eternity. Continuous non-existence does not require a cause (مقتضى) for its continuation. When the preponderating motive for existence arises, it is preferable for the act to come into existence, though it could still remain non-existent. The author clarifies that the capacity (القدرة) is not preponderated solely by the motive for omission (داعية الترك); rather, the continuation of non-existence is by itself, without need for a preponderator. Thus, the supposed impossibility is removed.

Key Terms
مغالطة
العدم المستمر
المقتضى
الداعية المرجحة
القدرة
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every change, including the transition from non-existence to existence, requires a preponderating cause.
Correction: The author argues that continuous non-existence does not require a cause; only the transition to existence may involve a preponderating motive, but even then it is not necessary for the act to exist.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does continuous non-existence not need a requisite?
A) Because it is impossible for something to remain non-existent.
B) Because the preponderating motive always brings it into existence.
C) Because it is self-subsistent and does not require an external cause for its continuation.
D) Because the requisite is only needed for existence, not non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Continuous non-existence is self-sustaining; only the transition to existence may require a preponderating motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a prolonged difficulty or lack in your life that you have been trying to explain or justify. How does this lesson's assertion that 'continuous non-existence does not need a cause' challenge your assumption that everything must have a reason? Can you accept that Allah may sustain a state of non-existence without any deficiency on His part? What does that teach you about your own need to find explanations for His decrees?
?
The author says that when a preponderating motive for existence arises, it is 'more appropriate' for the thing to exist, but it is not impossible for it to remain non-existent. Reflect on a blessing you have been waiting for—a prayer answered, a goal achieved. Are you truly convinced that Allah's withholding it is not due to a missing cause in you, but simply because He wills its continuation in non-existence? How does this realization humble your sense of entitlement before Allah?
?
Capacity (qudra) to act is not preponderated by a motive for omission. Consider your own inactions—the good deeds you leave undone, the sins you commit by omission. Do you often attribute them to external circumstances or lack of motivation? This lesson teaches that inaction itself does not need a cause; it simply is. Will you stop excusing your inactions and instead confront your capacity as fully responsible before Allah? What is one specific omission you will repent from today?

Two Aspects Distinguishing Natural from Voluntary Causes
Two aspects distinguishing natural from voluntary causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key differences between natural and voluntary causation: constancy of natural causes vs mutability of motives, and the role of consciousness in voluntary actions.

Core Idea
Natural causes are constant and unconscious; voluntary causes depend on changing motives and conscious deliberation.

The author sets out to clarify the fundamental differences between natural causes (like fire heating) and voluntary actions (like choosing to move). He gives two precise aspects: one concerning constancy, the other concerning consciousness. This lesson unpacks both aspects and shows why they matter for understanding free action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own language set the stage.
Notice the formal, analytical style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the first aspect
Find where the author discusses constancy. Natural causes remain in a fixed state, whereas voluntary actions depend on motives that change quickly.
The author gives the example of heating and cooling.
3
Identify the second aspect
Now look for the role of consciousness. Natural causes act without awareness, but voluntary agents require knowledge and perception of benefit or harm.
This aspect leads to the definition of 'voluntary' (ikhtiyārī) and 'free' (mukhtār).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these two aspects are important. They help explain why human actions are attributed to choice rather than pure determinism.
This connects to broader debates on free will and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المؤثرات الاختيارية. فنقول: هذا الفرق حاصل من وجهين:
الأول: أن المؤثرات الطبيعية، دائمة مستمرة غير مغيرة. فالمؤثر في التسخين يبقى أبدا موصوفا بالوجه الذي لأجله كان موجبا للتسخين، ولا يتغير. وكذا القول فيها يؤثر بالتبريد. بخلاف الأفعال الاختيارية. فإن الموجب للحركة بمنة: هو مجموع القدرة مع حصول الداعي إلى الحركة يسرة. وهذه الدواعي سريعة التبدل والتغير. فالقادر إذا حصلت في قلبه داعية الحركة يمنة، صار ذلك المجموع موجبا للحركة يمنة. ثم إن تلك الداعية تزول سريعا وتتبدل بحصول الداعية إلى الحركة يسرة. فيصير الآن هذا المجموع موجبا للحركة يسرة.
والحاصل أن في الأمور الطبيعية ما لأجله صار الشيء موجبا للتسخين يبقى ولا يتغير. وأما في الأمور الاختيارية فتلك الاختيارات سريعة التبدل والتغير. فهذا هو أحد الفريقين.
والوجه الثاني في الفرق: إن المؤثرات الطبيعية لا شعور لها بآثارها، ولا علم، ولا إدراك. وأما المؤثرات الاختيارية فهي إنما تؤثر بواسطة العلم والشعور والإدراك. فإنه ما لم يحصل تصور كون ذلك الفعل مفضيا إلى حصول اللذيذ، أو النافع لم يفعل. وما لم يحصل تصور كونه مفضيا إلى حصول المؤلم والضار لم يترك. ولهذا السبب: سمي هذا الفعل فعلا اختياريا، وهذا الفاعل مختارا. لأنه إنما يفعل إذا تصور خيرا. فلأجل أن هذا الفعل لا يحصل إلا عند تصور الخير، سمي هذا الفعل اختياريا، وسمي هذا الفاعل مختارا.
فظهر من هذين الوجهين: حصول الفرق بين المؤثرات الطبيعية، وبين المؤثرات الاختيارية.
The voluntary causes. We say: This difference is obtained from two aspects:
The first: That natural causes are constant, continuous, unchanging. So the cause in heating remains forever described by the aspect due to which it was necessitating heating, and does not change. And likewise the statement concerning what effects cooling. Opposite to voluntary actions. For that which necessitates motion to the right is the combination of ability (qudra) with the presence of the motive (dāʿī) to motion to the left. And these motives are quick to change and alter. So when a capable agent has a motive for motion to the right in his heart, that combination becomes necessitating motion to the right. Then that motive quickly disappears and changes by the occurrence of the motive to motion to the left. So now this combination becomes necessitating motion to the left.
The conclusion is that in natural matters, that due to which a thing becomes necessitating heating remains and does not change. But in voluntary matters, those choices are quick to change and alter. So this is one of the two aspects.
The second aspect of the difference: That natural causes have no consciousness of their effects, nor knowledge, nor perception. As for voluntary causes, they only effect through knowledge, consciousness, and perception. For unless there occurs a conception that that action leads to obtaining the pleasant or the beneficial, it is not done. And unless there occurs a conception that it leads to obtaining the painful and harmful, it is not abandoned. For this reason, this action is called voluntary (ikhtiyārī) and this agent is called free (mukhtār). Because he only acts when he conceives good. Because this action only occurs upon the conception of good, this action is called voluntary, and this agent is called free.
So it becomes clear from these two aspects: the existence of the difference between natural causes and voluntary causes.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the difference between natural causes (al-mu'aththirāt al-ṭabīʿiyya) and voluntary actions (al-afʿāl al-ikhtiyāriyya) through two aspects. The author first states the conclusion: the difference is obtained from two aspects.

First aspect: Natural causes are constant and unchanging. For example, a heating agent always remains in the condition that makes it necessitate heating and never changes. Similarly, a cooling agent. In contrast, voluntary actions depend on motives (dawāʿī) which are quickly changing. A capable agent (al-qādir) combines ability (qudra) with a motive; when the motive changes, the action changes direction. So the first difference is that natural causes are stable while motives are mutable.

Second aspect: Natural causes have no consciousness (shuʿūr) of their effects, nor knowledge (ʿilm), nor perception (idrāk). Voluntary agents, however, act through knowledge, consciousness, and perception. An action is only performed if there is a conception (taṣawwur) that it leads to pleasure or benefit, and it is only abandoned if conceived leading to pain or harm. Thus the action is called 'ikhtiyārī' (voluntary) and the agent 'mukhtār' (free) because he only acts upon conceiving something good.

The author then concludes that from these two aspects the difference is established.

Key Terms
المؤثرات الطبيعية
المؤثرات الاختيارية
الدواعي
الإرادة
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only difference between natural and voluntary causes is that one is physical and the other is mental.
Correction: The author identifies two specific aspects: constancy/mutability of the cause and presence/absence of consciousness. The difference is not merely physical vs mental but about stability and awareness.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect, what distinguishes natural causes from voluntary actions?
A) Natural causes are constant while motives behind voluntary actions are changeable.
B) Voluntary actions are constant while natural causes change.
C) Both are equally changeable.
D) Natural causes involve consciousness.

Key Takeaway
Natural causes are constant and unconscious, while voluntary causes are mutable and depend on conscious motives—these two aspects fundamentally distinguish them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a voluntary agent acts only after conceiving benefit or harm. Look at your last major decision—did you truly conceive its ultimate benefit, or did you act out of habit or impulse? What would it mean for you to become more conscious in your choices?
?
Your motives are described as 'quick to change and alter.' When you find yourself inconsistent in your commitments, do you blame circumstances, or do you admit that your heart's motives are fickle? Which aspect of your character needs strengthening to align your motives with what is truly good?
?
Natural causes never change; they are steady and predictable. But you are not a natural cause—you are a voluntary agent. In what area of your life do you wish to become more constant, like a natural cause, in pursuing good? What would you need to change to achieve that constancy?

The Objection Confirms Divine Causation
Author argues that the objection about the motive confirms his position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection about the motive actually confirms the author's position on divine causation of human action.

Core Idea
The objection is not a refutation but a confirmation of divine causation.

The author has established that human action depends on a motive placed by God. Now an objector raises a concern. In this passage, the author turns the objection on its head, showing it actually proves his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the key terms 'al-da'iya' and 'al-tasalsul'.
2
Identify the author's original position
The author states that an action depends on a specific motive occurring in the heart of the agent.
This is the premise that the objector challenges.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
If the motive came from the servant, it would require another motive, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, it must come from God.
This is the core logical move.
4
See how the objection confirms the position
The objector's concern actually reinforces the author's conclusion that the servant is not independent. The author dismisses the objection as stemming from poor understanding.
The objection is turned into a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأنا بينا: أن صدور الفعل عن القادر يتوقف على حصول الداعية المخصوصة في قلبه. وحصول تلك الداعية بعينها في قلبه، ليس بداعية أخرى من قبل العبد وإلا لزم التسلل. بل لا بد وأن يكون من الله، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العبد غير مستقل لا بالفعل ولا بالترك. فهذا الذي أوردتموه علينا ليس اعتراضا على كلامنا بل هو تأكيد، وتقوية، وتقرير له فظهر أن هذا السؤال الذي ذكروه: سؤال ساقط، لا يصدر إلا عن قلة الفهم
And that is because we explained: that the occurrence of the act from the capable (agent) depends on the occurrence of the specific motive (al-da'iya al-makhsusa) in his heart. And the occurrence of that same motive in his heart is not another motive from the servant, otherwise it would lead to infinite regress (al-tasalsul). Rather, it must be from God, and then it follows that the servant is not independent either in the act or in the abandonment. So this which you have brought against us is not an objection to our speech, but rather it is a confirmation, a strengthening, and an establishment of it. So it became clear that this question which they mentioned is a worthless question; it arises only from lack of understanding.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The author begins by stating his own position: that an action from a capable agent depends on the occurrence of a specific motive (da'iya) in the heart. This motive cannot come from the servant himself, as that would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul); it must come from God. Therefore, the servant is not independent in either doing or leaving an act. The objector claimed this leads to problems, but the author argues that this objection actually confirms his position—it is not a refutation but an acknowledgment. Hence, the question is invalid and stems from poor comprehension.

Key Terms
الداعية (al-da'iya)
التسلل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the motive came from the servant?
A) it would make the servant independent
B) it would lead to infinite regress
C) it would disprove divine power
D) it would prove human free will

Key Takeaway
The objection that the motive comes from God actually strengthens the argument that human action depends entirely on divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
