6 min
The Impossibility of an Act Without a Preponderating Factor
Author refutes the claim that an act can occur without any preponderating factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an act cannot occur without a preponderating factor, as demonstrated by essential human experience and rational intuition.

Core Idea
Every act requires a preponderating factor; otherwise, action is impossible.

The author presents the first of two possible claims: that a capable agent can act without any preponderating factor. He then refutes it by showing that even in ordinary human experience, balanced motives lead to inaction until a preponderance appears. This lesson examines this refutation and its reliance on necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice that it begins by stating the two claims and then immediately declares the first false.
The author uses a real-world example to prove his point.
2
Analyze the market example
The author describes a person walking in the market who considers turning back. When the two motives are balanced, the person remains motionless. Only when one side becomes more beneficial does he move. This shows that a preponderating factor is necessary for action.
This is an appeal to necessary knowledge (badiha).
3
Consider the thirsty person example
The author gives a second example: a thirsty person with two cups. If he does not intend to take any, it is absurd to think one cup would rise to his mouth by itself. This reinforces the necessity of a preponderating factor.
4
Reflect on the rational intuition
The author concludes that the claim of action without a murajjih is repudiated by the intuition of reason. Reflect on how these examples make the necessity self-evident.
You should now see why the first claim is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في الجواب: إنكم إما أن تقولوا: إن القادر على الضدين، يترجح صدور أحد الضدين عنه من غير مرجح البتة. أو تقولوا: القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الثاني من غير مرجح. والأول باطل. لأنا نجد أنفسنا بالضرورة: أن الذي يمشي في السوق إلى موضع، إذا وقع في قلبه: أن الرجوع عن ذلك الجانب إلى جانب آخر: مصلحة من وجه. فإنه متى تعادلت الداعيتان في قلبه، ولم يترجح أحدهما على الآخر البتة، فإن ذلك الإنسان يبقى في ذلك الموضع، ولا يمكنه أن يتقدم أو يتأخر ويبقى على تلك الحالة إلى أن يقع في قلبه: أن أحد الجانبين أكثر مصلحة. وعند ظهور هذا الرجحان يترجح الإذهاب إلى ذلك الجانب. فثبت: أن القول بأنه يصدر عنه أحد الضدين من غير أن يكون هو مرجحا لذلك الضد كلام تدفعه بديهة العقل. وأيضا: فهل يقبل العقل أن يقال: العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين، ثم أنه لا يمد يده إلى أحدهما، ولا يخص أحدهما بالأخذ والرفع. بل المجرد كونه قادرا يرتفع أحد القدحين إلى فمه، وينصب ذلك الماء في حلقه؟ فهذا مما يعلم بطلانه ببديهة العقل. وأما إن قيل: إن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لمرجح. فنقول: هذا الكلام في نفسه متناقض. لأن هذا القائل سلم أن ذلك القادر ما لم يرجح أحد ذينك المقدورين على الآخر، لم يحصل الرجحان وسلم أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادرا. فإذا كان لا يحصل الرجحان إلا عند حصول ذلك الترجيح، وثبت أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادر، فهذا اعتراف بأن الرجحان لا يحصل إلا عند المرجح. فقوله: القادر يرجح، مشعر بحصول المرجح الزائد. وقوله بعد ذلك: "إنه لا لمرجح"، مشعر بنفي المرجح الزائد، فكان قوله: (القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لمرجح) جمع بين النقيضين. وهو كلام باطل.
The second aspect of the answer: You either say that the capable (agent) over two opposites (al-qadir 'ala al-diddayn) has one of the two opposites emanate from him without any preponderating factor (murajjih) at all, or you say that the capable (agent) preponderates (yurajjih) one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor. The first is false. For we find in ourselves necessarily that one who walks in the market to a place, when it occurs in his heart that returning from that side to another side is beneficial in some respect, then whenever the two motives are balanced in his heart, and neither of them preponderates over the other at all, that person remains in that place and cannot move forward or backward, and he remains in that state until it occurs in his heart that one of the two sides is more beneficial. And upon the appearance of this preponderance, the going to that side becomes preponderant. So it is established that the statement that one of the two opposites emanates from him without him being a preponderator of that opposite is a statement repudiated by the intuition of reason. Also, does reason accept that it be said: a thirsty person, if given a choice between drinking two cups, and then he does not extend his hand to either one, nor does he single out one for taking and raising, but rather, merely by virtue of being capable, one of the two cups is raised to his mouth and that water is poured into his throat? This is known to be false by the intuition of reason. And as for if it is said that the capable (agent) preponderates one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor, we say: This statement is in itself contradictory. For this speaker conceded that that capable (agent), as long as he does not preponderate one of those two possible acts over the other, preponderance does not occur, and he conceded that this preponderating (tarjih) is something additional to mere capability. So if preponderance does not occur except upon the occurrence of that preponderating, and it is established that that preponderating is something additional to mere capability, then this is an acknowledgment that preponderance does not occur except with the preponderating factor. So his saying 'the capable preponderates' indicates the occurrence of the additional preponderating factor, and his saying afterward 'without a preponderating factor' indicates the negation of the additional preponderating factor. Thus his statement 'the capable preponderates one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor' combines two contradictories, and it is an invalid statement.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second aspect of an objection. The author presents two possible claims. First claim: the capable agent can produce one of two opposites without any preponderating factor (murajjih). The author refutes this by appealing to empirical observation: when a person has two balanced motives, they remain motionless until a preponderance occurs. Therefore, an act cannot occur without a murajjih. He gives the example of a thirsty person choosing between two cups: it is absurd to think the cup would rise to his mouth without his intending it. Second claim: the agent preponderates without a murajjih. The author points out a contradiction: if the agent preponderates, that preponderating (tarjih) is an additional factor, but then claiming 'without a murajjih' negates that factor. Thus the statement combines two contradictories and is invalid.

Key Terms
القادر على الضدين (al-qadir 'ala al-diddayn)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
الداعية (al-da'iya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an agent is capable, he can act without any reason or preference.
Correction: The author shows that even in ordinary experience, action requires a preponderating factor; otherwise, the agent remains motionless.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens when the two motives in the market example are balanced?
A) The person moves forward
B) The person remains motionless
C) The person turns back
D) The person acts randomly

Key Takeaway
An act cannot occur without a preponderating factor; this is evident from necessary human experience.

?
The thirsty man with two cups is absurd—yet how often do you claim 'I just felt like it' as a reason for your actions? Are you ignoring the real preponderating factors (desires, fears, habits) that drive you? This lesson demands you stop hiding behind vague intentions and examine the actual 'murajjih' in your life. What would you find?

If every act requires a preponderating factor, then your prayer, your charity, your patience—what is truly making them preponderate in your heart? Is it love of Allah or something else? Be honest: if the motive for worship is weak, you will remain stagnant. What will you do today to strengthen the preponderating factor of faith in your heart?

The Contradiction in Preponderating Without a Factor
Author refutes the claim that preponderating can occur without a preponderating factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that an agent preponderates without a preponderating factor is contradictory and rationally invalid.

Core Idea
To preponderate is to introduce a preponderating factor, so saying 'without a factor' is self-contradictory.

The author now addresses the second claim: that the agent preponderates one possible act over another without any preponderating factor. He shows that this statement combines two contradictories, because the very act of preponderating (tarjih) is an additional factor. This lesson examines the logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second refutation. Notice how the author identifies a contradiction.
The author uses careful logical analysis.
2
Identify the two parts of the claim
The claim says 'the capable preponderates' and 'without a preponderating factor'. The author shows that 'preponderating' itself implies an additional factor.
This is the key to the contradiction.
3
Understand the logical argument
If preponderating is additional to mere capability, then saying 'without a factor' negates that addition. Thus the statement simultaneously affirms and denies the additional factor, which is contradictory.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the statement combines two contradictories and is invalid. Reflect on why this refutes the second claim completely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في الجواب: إنكم إما أن تقولوا: إن القادر على الضدين، يترجح صدور أحد الضدين عنه من غير مرجح البتة. أو تقولوا: القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الثاني من غير مرجح. والأول باطل. لأنا نجد أنفسنا بالضرورة: أن الذي يمشي في السوق إلى موضع، إذا وقع في قلبه: أن الرجوع عن ذلك الجانب إلى جانب آخر: مصلحة من وجه. فإنه متى تعادلت الداعيتان في قلبه، ولم يترجح أحدهما على الآخر البتة، فإن ذلك الإنسان يبقى في ذلك الموضع، ولا يمكنه أن يتقدم أو يتأخر ويبقى على تلك الحالة إلى أن يقع في قلبه: أن أحد الجانبين أكثر مصلحة. وعند ظهور هذا الرجحان يترجح الإذهاب إلى ذلك الجانب. فثبت: أن القول بأنه يصدر عنه أحد الضدين من غير أن يكون هو مرجحا لذلك الضد كلام تدفعه بديهة العقل. وأيضا: فهل يقبل العقل أن يقال: العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين، ثم أنه لا يمد يده إلى أحدهما، ولا يخص أحدهما بالأخذ والرفع. بل المجرد كونه قادرا يرتفع أحد القدحين إلى فمه، وينصب ذلك الماء في حلقه؟ فهذا مما يعلم بطلانه ببديهة العقل. وأما إن قيل: إن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لمرجح. فنقول: هذا الكلام في نفسه متناقض. لأن هذا القائل سلم أن ذلك القادر ما لم يرجح أحد ذينك المقدورين على الآخر، لم يحصل الرجحان وسلم أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادرا. فإذا كان لا يحصل الرجحان إلا عند حصول ذلك الترجيح، وثبت أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادر، فهذا اعتراف بأن الرجحان لا يحصل إلا عند المرجح. فقوله: القادر يرجح، مشعر بحصول المرجح الزائد. وقوله بعد ذلك: "إنه لا لمرجح"، مشعر بنفي المرجح الزائد، فكان قوله: (القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لمرجح) جمع بين النقيضين. وهو كلام باطل.
The second aspect of the answer: You either say that the capable (agent) over two opposites (al-qadir 'ala al-diddayn) has one of the two opposites emanate from him without any preponderating factor (murajjih) at all, or you say that the capable (agent) preponderates (yurajjih) one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor. The first is false. For we find in ourselves necessarily that one who walks in the market to a place, when it occurs in his heart that returning from that side to another side is beneficial in some respect, then whenever the two motives are balanced in his heart, and neither of them preponderates over the other at all, that person remains in that place and cannot move forward or backward, and he remains in that state until it occurs in his heart that one of the two sides is more beneficial. And upon the appearance of this preponderance, the going to that side becomes preponderant. So it is established that the statement that one of the two opposites emanates from him without him being a preponderator of that opposite is a statement repudiated by the intuition of reason. Also, does reason accept that it be said: a thirsty person, if given a choice between drinking two cups, and then he does not extend his hand to either one, nor does he single out one for taking and raising, but rather, merely by virtue of being capable, one of the two cups is raised to his mouth and that water is poured into his throat? This is known to be false by the intuition of reason. And as for if it is said that the capable (agent) preponderates one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor, we say: This statement is in itself contradictory. For this speaker conceded that that capable (agent), as long as he does not preponderate one of those two possible acts over the other, preponderance does not occur, and he conceded that this preponderating (tarjih) is something additional to mere capability. So if preponderance does not occur except upon the occurrence of that preponderating, and it is established that that preponderating is something additional to mere capability, then this is an acknowledgment that preponderance does not occur except with the preponderating factor. So his saying 'the capable preponderates' indicates the occurrence of the additional preponderating factor, and his saying afterward 'without a preponderating factor' indicates the negation of the additional preponderating factor. Thus his statement 'the capable preponderates one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderating factor' combines two contradictories, and it is an invalid statement.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second aspect of an objection. The author presents two possible claims. First claim: the capable agent can produce one of two opposites without any preponderating factor (murajjih). The author refutes this by appealing to empirical observation: when a person has two balanced motives, they remain motionless until a preponderance occurs. Therefore, an act cannot occur without a murajjih. He gives the example of a thirsty person choosing between two cups: it is absurd to think the cup would rise to his mouth without his intending it. Second claim: the agent preponderates without a murajjih. The author points out a contradiction: if the agent preponderates, that preponderating (tarjih) is an additional factor, but then claiming 'without a murajjih' negates that factor. Thus the statement combines two contradictories and is invalid.

Key Terms
المرجح (al-murajjih)
الترجيح (al-tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'preponderating' is just an activity that does not require an additional factor.
Correction: The author shows that preponderating (tarjih) is itself a factor beyond mere capability, so claiming it occurs without a factor is contradictory.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the contradiction in the claim 'the capable preponderates without a preponderating factor'?
A) It denies the existence of the agent
B) It combines affirming and denying an additional factor
C) It assumes the agent is not capable
D) It confuses preponderance with capability

Key Takeaway
The claim of preponderating without a factor is self-contradictory because preponderating itself is an additional factor.

The First Proof: Act's Likelihood Necessitates Occurrence
Sanusi presents the first proof against the fifth objection and refutes a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the claim that an act can become more likely to occur without actually occurring, and why non-existence cannot be self-sustaining for a possible being.

Core Idea
If an act is more likely, it must occur; otherwise it becomes impossible.

Sanusi presents the first proof against the objection that an act, upon the motive, becomes more likely without occurring. He argues that if it were merely more likely, it would require a cause for its non-existence, leading to impossibility. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the technical terms like al-awlawiyya, al-marjuha, al-rajih, and al-mumkin.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Follow the first proof from equality to impossibility
Identify the chain: state of equality → prevented → less likely → impossible → more likely must be necessary. See how the author uses this to refute the objection.
The proof avoids infinite regress by making the less likely side impossible.
3
Understand the refutation of the counterpoint
The objector claims non-existence continues by default. Sanusi refutes by showing that a possible being cannot have self-sustaining non-existence; it would make it necessary or independent of cause.
This reinforces the need for a cause for both existence and non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس: وهو بقوله: «لا يجوز أن يقال: الفعل عند الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، مع أنه لا ينتهي إلى الوقوف، فنقول: هذا باطل ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: ما ذكرنا: أنه حال الاستواء كان ممنوع الوقوع، فحال المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع، وإذا كان طرف المرجوح ممتنعا، كان الراجح واجبا. لأنه لا خروج عن التقصير. قوله: (إن بقاءه على العدم، لا يكون لأجل حصول الداعي إلى الترك، بل لأجل أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان)، قلنا: هذا محال. لأن الممكن هو الذي يكون دائرا بين الوجود وبين العدم. فكما أن وجوده يتعلل بوجود ما يقتضي الوجود، فعدمه معلل بعدم ذلك المؤثر. فإما أن يقال إنه يستمر عدمه لنفسه. فذلك محال. لأنه يقتضي، إما إنقلاب الممكن واجبا، وإما استغناء الممكن عن المؤثر. وكلاهما محال.
As for the fifth objection: it is his statement: 'It is not permissible to say: the act, upon the motive, becomes more likely (awla) to occur, while it does not end up occurring.' We say: This is false, and evidence for it is [several] aspects. The first proof: what we mentioned: that in the state of equality (istawa'), occurrence was prevented (mamnu'), so the state of being less likely (marjuha) is closer to impossibility. And when the side of the less likely (marjuh) is impossible, then the more likely (rajih) is necessary, because there is no escape from [the two extremes]. His statement: 'Its remaining in non-existence is not due to the occurrence of the motive for omission, but because the original state of every matter is its remaining as it was,' we say: This is impossible. For the possible (mumkin) is that which is between existence and non-existence. Just as its existence is caused by the existence of what necessitates existence, so its non-existence is caused by the non-existence of that effect. For if it is said that its non-existence continues by itself, that is impossible, because it necessitates either the transformation of the possible into necessary, or the independence of the possible from the effect. Both are impossible.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses the fifth objection. The objector claims it is not valid to say that an act becomes 'more likely' (awla) to occur upon the presence of the motive (da'iya) without actually occurring. The author states this is false and provides a first proof. He argues: In a state of equal motives (istawa'), occurrence is already prevented (mamnu'). If it is prevented in equality, then in a state where the motive is weaker (marjuh), the act is even closer to impossibility. Therefore, if the weaker side is impossible, then the stronger side (rajih) must be necessary. This avoids an infinite regress. Then he refutes a counterpoint: The objector says the act's non-existence is not due to a motive for omission but because the original state is continuance in non-existence. The author rejects this, explaining that a possible (mumkin) being requires a cause for its existence and a cause for its non-existence. If non-existence were self-sustaining, it would either turn the possible into necessary or make it independent of the cause—both impossible.

Key Terms
الأولوية (al-awlawiyya)
المرجوحية (al-marjuha)
الراجح (al-rajih)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act can become 'more likely' without a cause, and its non-existence can continue by default without a cause for omission.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if the act is more likely, it must be necessary, and non-existence cannot be self-sustaining; both require a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the state of the less likely (marjuh) side?
A) necessary
B) impossible
C) possible
D) contingent

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if an act is more likely, it must be necessary, and non-existence cannot be self-sustaining for a possible being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the act is truly more likely (awla) in your heart, why does it not happen? Are you truly motivated, or are you deceiving yourself about the strength of your motive? Commit to examining one such act today and act upon it immediately.
?
The refutation says that non-existence for a possible being cannot persist by itself; it requires a cause. Consider your own spiritual state: when you neglect worship, do you assume that your negligence is simply the 'original state' of your soul? Or are you avoiding the uncomfortable truth that your non-action has a cause—perhaps a hidden desire or fear? What would it take for you to remove that cause and let the more likely good deed become necessary in your life?
?
If an act is more likely (rajih), it must occur; otherwise, it is impossible. Think of a persistent sin you struggle with but know you should abandon. According to this proof, if abandoning it were truly more likely, you would have done so. Why have you not? Is it because you have not genuinely deemed it more likely? What would change if you truly convinced yourself that obedience to Allah is the only necessary, possible path? Write down one step you will take today to tip the balance.

Refutation of a Self-Contradictory Statement on Preponderance
Al-Matalib refutes a contradictory claim that the qadir preponderates without a murajjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author identifies and refutes a self-contradictory statement about preponderance (tarjih) being possible without a preponderator (murajjih).

Core Idea
A claim that preponderance occurs without a preponderator collapses into logical contradiction.

In this passage, al-Matalib exposes a logical flaw in an opponent's argument about preponderance. The opponent tries to assert that a capable agent can preponderate one possible act over another without any additional factor, but the author shows that this very statement contradicts itself. By analyzing the opponent's own premises, the author reveals that his words combine affirmation and negation of the same thing, rendering his claim false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the opponent's claim that the capable agent (qadir) preponderates without a preponderator (murajjih).
Notice the author begins by calling the statement contradictory.
2
Identify the opponent's admissions
The author points out two things the opponent has admitted: (1) preponderance (tarjih) does not occur unless the qadir preponderates, and (2) this tarjih is something additional to mere capability (qudra). Understand why these admissions are crucial.
The opponent himself acknowledges that tarjih is an extra factor.
3
See how the contradiction arises
If tarjih is additional, then saying 'the qadir preponderates' implies the existence of that additional preponderator. But the opponent then says 'without a preponderator,' which denies it. This is a combination of two contradictories.
The phrase 'qadir yurajjih' already suggests a murajjih, but 'la li-murajjih' counters it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : هذا الكلام في نفسه متناقض . لأن هذا القائل سلم أن ذلك القادر ما لم يرجح أحد ذينك المقدورين على الآخر ، لم يحصل الرجحان وسلم أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادرا . فإذا كان لا يحصل الرجحان إلا عند حصول ذلك الترجيح ، وثبت أن ذلك الترجيح أمر زائد على مجرد كونه قادر ، فهذا اعتراف بأن الرجحان لا يحصل إلا عند المرجح . فقوله : القادر يرجح ، مشعر بحصول المرجح الزائد. وقوله بعد ذلك : « إنه لا لمرجح ، مشعر بنفى المرجح الزائد، فكان قوله : ( القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لمرجح ، جمع بين النقيضين . وهو كلام باطل
So we say: This statement in itself is contradictory. Because this speaker acknowledged that that capable agent (al-qadir), as long as he does not preponderate one of those two possible acts over the other, preponderance (al-tarjih) does not occur, and he acknowledged that this preponderance is something additional to his mere being capable. So if preponderance does not occur except when that preponderance occurs, and it is established that this preponderance is something additional to his mere being capable, then this is an admission that preponderance does not occur except with the preponderator (al-murajjih). So his saying 'the capable agent preponderates' indicates the occurrence of the additional preponderator. And his saying afterwards 'without a preponderator' indicates the negation of the additional preponderator. So his statement 'the capable agent preponderates one of his two possible acts over the other without a preponderator' is a combination of two contradictories, and it is false speech.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a contradictory statement. The author shows the opponent's claim that the capable agent (qadir) can preponderate one possible act over another without any preponderator (murajjih) is self-contradictory. The author points out that the opponent himself admits that preponderance (tarjih) is something additional to mere capability. Therefore, if preponderance only occurs with a preponderator, then saying 'the qadir preponderates' implies a preponderator, while saying 'without a preponderator' denies it. Thus the statement combines two contradictories and is false.

Key Terms
قادر
ترجيح
مرجح
مقدور
نقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the capable agent's power alone is sufficient for preponderance, and that no additional factor is needed.
Correction: This passage shows that the opponent's own admission that tarjih is additional to qudra implies that a preponderator must exist, making the claim of preponderance without a preponderator self-contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) Because the qadir cannot preponderate at all.
B) Because the opponent both posits and denies an additional preponderator.
C) Because the qadir's power is infinite.
D) Because preponderance is impossible for possible acts.

Key Takeaway
The statement that a capable agent preponderates without a preponderator is self-contradictory because it both affirms and denies the existence of a preponderator within the same sentence.

The Fifth Question: Why a Motive Cannot Make an Act 'More Likely' Without Actualization
Al-Matalib presents the fifth question, the first proof, and an objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the claim that a motive makes an act 'more likely' without occurring, and the refutation of the objection that non-existence needs no cause.

Core Idea
If non-existence required no cause, every possible would be independent of Allah.

In this passage, we encounter a critical question in Islamic occasionalist ontology: can a motive (da'i) make an action more likely (awla) without it actually happening? The author rejects this and provides the first of several proofs. He then addresses a potential objection about the default state of non-existence. This lesson unpacks that argument and its refutation, clarifying why every possible—whether existing or not—requires a cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of the passage
Read the passage once and note the three main sections: the statement of the fifth question, the first proof (al-hujja al-ula), and the objection-with-response.
The objection begins with 'قوله' and the refutation with 'قلنا'.
2
Understand the first proof's logic
The proof uses the state of equality (istiwa'). If occurrence was impossible at equality (since nothing necessitates it), then the less likely side (marjuh) is even more impossible. Therefore, the preponderant side (rajih) becomes necessary (wajib), as there is no third option.
This is a purely rational argument grounded in the three rational judgments.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
An opponent argues that non-existence continues because it's the original state, not due to a motive to refrain. The author refutes: the possible is equally open to existence and non-existence, so both require a cause. If non-existence continued by itself, the possible would become necessary or independent of the cause—both absurd.
This refutation ties the proof back to the principle of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس : وهو بقوله : « لا يجوز أن يقال : الفعل عند الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع ، مع أنه لا ينتهي إلى الوقوف ، فنقول : هذا باطل ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : ما ذكرنا : أنه حال الاستواء كان [ ممنوع ] الوقوع ، فحال المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع ، وإذا كان طرف المرجوح ممتنعا ، كان الراجح واجبا . لأنه لا خروج عن التقصير . قوله : ( إن بقاءه على العدم ، لا يكون لأجل حصول الداعي إلى الترك ، بل لأجل أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان ، قلنا : هذا محال . لأن الممكن هو الذي يكون [ دائرا ] بين الوجود وبين العدم. فكما أن وجوده يتعلل بوجود ما يقتضي الوجود ، فعدمه معلل بعدم ذلك المؤثر. فإما أن يقال إنه يستمر عدمه لنفسه . فذلك محال . لأنه يقتضي ، إما إنقلاب الممكن واجبا ، وإما استغناء الممكن عن المؤثر . وكلاهما محال
As for the fifth question: It is his saying: 'It is not permissible to say that the act, with the motive (al-da'i), becomes more likely to occur, while it does not come to a stop.' So we say: This is false, and it is indicated by proofs: The first proof: What we mentioned: that in the state of equality (al-istiwa'), occurrence was impossible, so the state of being less likely (al-marjuhiyya) is more deserving of impossibility. And if the less likely side is impossible, then the preponderant (al-rajih) is necessary (wajib), because there is no escape from the deficiency. His saying: 'That its remaining in non-existence is not due to the occurrence of a motive to refrain, but because the original state of everything is its remaining as it was.' We say: This is impossible. Because the possible (al-mumkin) is that which is [da'ir] between existence and non-existence. Just as its existence is caused by the existence of that which necessitates existence, so its non-existence is caused by the non-existence of that effect. So either it is said that it continues in non-existence by itself, but that is impossible, because it would require either the possible becoming necessary or the possible being independent of the cause. Both are impossible.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth question and provides the first proof. The question denies that an act with a motive (da'i) becomes 'more likely' (awla) to occur without actually occurring. The author declares it false and gives proofs. The first proof uses the state of equality (istiwa'): if at equality occurrence was impossible, then at the less likely state (marjuhiyya) it is even more impossible, so the preponderant side (rajih) becomes necessary (wajib). The author then addresses an objection: someone says non-existence continues not due to a motive to refrain but because the original state remains. The author refutes this: the possible (mumkin) is between existence and non-existence; both are caused. If non-existence continued by itself, that would imply the possible becoming necessary or independent of the cause—both impossible.

ممكن
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is the default state of a possible and requires no cause; it continues by itself.
Correction: The possible (al-mumkin) is equally in need of a cause for its non-existence as for its existence; non-existence is caused by the absence of the effect (al-mu'aththir).

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what happens to the less likely side (al-marjuh) if the state of equality (al-istiwa') already made occurrence impossible?
A) It becomes possible.
B) It becomes necessary.
C) It becomes even more impossible.
D) It remains possible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that a motive cannot make an act 'more likely' without actualizing it, and even non-existence requires a cause, preserving the principle that every possible depends on Allah.

?
If every possible good deed requires a cause for its non-existence, what must change about how you view your own laziness or procrastination? Are you willing to trace your omissions back to the missing 'mu'aththir' in your heart, or do you hide behind the comfort of 'just not doing it'?
?
This lesson shows that the original state argument is an illusion—non-existence is not self-evident. Where in your life do you treat your spiritual voids as naturally perpetual, and what step will you take today to seek the cause that would transform your lack into existence?

Second Proof: The Impossibility of Preference Without Preponderator
A dilemma argument against 'awlawiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against 'awlawiyya, which uses a dilemma to show that any distinction in existence or non-existence requires a preponderator.

Core Idea
A degree of preponderance cannot by itself explain why existence occurs at one time and not another.

This lesson explores Al-Matalib's second proof against the doctrine of 'awlawiyya (priority). The argument presents a dilemma: when a degree of preponderance is obtained from capability and motive, either non-existence becomes impossible (so existence is necessary) or it remains possible. The latter case leads to either a preponderance without a preponderator or an infinite regress. By following this reasoning, you will see why the mutakallimun insist on the necessity of a real preponderator for any actual occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Note the key terms: قدرة, رجحان, زمان, تسلسل.
The passage is a dense logical argument; read slowly.
2
Identify the main dilemma
The author sets up a dichotomy: when a degree of preponderance is obtained, either non-existence is impossible or it is not. Identify these two branches.
3
Analyze the first horn
If non-existence is impossible, then existence is necessary—that is the desired conclusion. This path is straightforward.
4
Analyze the second horn
If non-existence is not impossible, then we suppose it occurs. Then consider two sub-cases: (a) no additional condition needed, (b) additional condition needed. Each leads to absurdity.
This is the core of the proof.
5
Understand the conclusions
Both sub-cases lead to impossibility: either preponderance without a preponderator or infinite regress. Therefore, the initial assumption that non-existence is possible with that degree of preponderance is false.
Thus the degree of preponderance must make non-existence impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بطلان هذه الأولوية : أن نقول : لاشك أن عند حصول القدرة مع الداعية المرجحة ، قد حصل قدر من الرجحان . فنقول : عند حصول ذلك القدر من الرجحان . إن امتنع العدم، فقد حصل الوجود . وذلك هو المطلوب . وإن لم يمتنع العدم، فلنفرض ذلك واقعا . فإن كل ما كان ممكنا ، لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون قد حصل عند حصول ذلك القدر من الرجحان الوجود تارة والعدم أخرى بامتياز أحد الوقتين عن الآخر . بحصول الأثر في أحدهما ، وعدم حصوله في الثاني ، إن لم يتوقف على انضمام قيد إليه فقد يرجح الممكن المتساوي لا عن مرجح نسبة ذلك القدر من الرجحان إلى الوقتين، لما كان على السوية ، ولم يختص أحد الوقتين بمزية ، لأجله صار هو أولى بالوقوع ، فحينئذ يكون تمييز أحد الوقتين عن الثاني تمييزا لأحد طرفي الممكن [ المتساوي ] عن الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وإما أن يتوقف على انضمام قيد إليه ، فحينئذ يكون المرجح هو المجموع الحاصل من الأمر الذي كان حاصلا ، مع انضمام هذا القيد إليه فيلزم أن يقال : إن مجرد الأمر الذي كان حاصلا [ قبل ] ذلك ، ما كان تمام المرجح . مع انا فرضناه تمام المرجح . هذا خلف . وأيضا : يتحقق بقيد التقسيم عند حصول ذلك المجموع . فنقول : إن كان الفعل [ واجبا ] فهو المقصود . وإن لم يكن واجبا ، عاد التقسيم فيه . ولزم التسلسل . وهو محال
The second proof for the invalidity of this 'awlawiyya: We say: There is no doubt that when capability (al-qudra) together with the preponderating motive is obtained, a certain degree of preponderance has been obtained. So we say: When that degree of preponderance is obtained, if non-existence is impossible, then existence has occurred, and that is what is sought. And if non-existence is not impossible, then let us suppose it occurs. For everything that is possible, it is not impossible to suppose its occurrence. On this supposition, it would be that with the occurrence of that degree of preponderance, existence occurs sometimes and non-existence other times, by one time being distinguished from another. If it does not depend on an additional condition, then the possible that is equal would be preponderated without a preponderator, because that degree of preponderance is equally related to both times, and no time has a special merit making it more likely to occur. Then distinguishing one time from another would be distinguishing one side of the possible [equal] from the other without a preponderator. And if it depends on an additional condition, then the preponderator would be the total from the thing that was obtained together with the addition of this condition. Then it would be necessary to say that the mere thing that was obtained [before] was not the complete preponderator, but we assumed it was the complete preponderator. This is absurd. Also, the division will be verified by the condition upon obtaining that total. So we say: If the act is [necessary], that is the purpose. If it is not necessary, the division returns to it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is the second proof. The author sets up a dilemma: when a degree of preponderance is obtained from capability (qudra) and motive (da'iya murajjiha), either non-existence is impossible (so existence is necessary) or it is not impossible. If not impossible, we suppose non-existence occurs with that same degree of preponderance. Then the distinction between one time having existence and another having non-existence must be explained. The author considers two cases: (1) If no additional condition is needed, then the preponderance is equally related to both times, so the distinction would be without a preponderator—impossible. (2) If an additional condition is needed, then the original factor was not the complete preponderator, contradicting the assumption. Moreover, when the condition is added, we again ask whether the act becomes necessary or not, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus all possibilities lead to impossibility.

Key Terms
قدرة
رجحان
زمان
تسلسل
ممكن متساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A degree of preponderance can explain why existence happens at one time and not another without any additional preponderator.
Correction: This proof shows that if the same degree of preponderance is equally related to both times, then distinguishing one time from another would be a preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, a real preponderator must be involved.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude if the occurrence of non-existence with the same degree of preponderance does not depend on an additional condition?
A) That the act becomes necessary.
B) That the possible would be preponderated without a preponderator.
C) That infinite regress follows.
D) That the initial factor was not complete.

Key Takeaway
The second proof against 'awlawiyya demonstrates that any distinction in the occurrence of an effect must be grounded in a preponderator; otherwise, absurdities like preponderance without a preponderator or infinite regress follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that any distinction in the occurrence of an effect must have a preponderator. Consider a situation in your life where you believe your own ability or effort made the difference at a particular time. How does this argument confront your assumption that you alone are the cause of your successes? Will you now trace every moment of achievement back to the One who truly preponderates?
?
The argument demonstrates that without a preponderator, preference is impossible. When you delay an action or procrastinate, do you ever consider that the time you choose to act is not merely a matter of your convenience but is determined by a preponderator? How does this challenge your sense of control over your own choices?
?
Infinite regress is deemed impossible. Reflect on a habit or belief in your life that you have never traced to its ultimate cause. Are you content with an endless chain of reasoning, or will you now seek the First Preponderator Who gives existence to all things?

Third Proof: The Impossibility of Simultaneous Preponderance
Al-Matalib's third proof against simultaneous preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why simultaneous preponderance of existence and non-existence is logically impossible, and how this argument supports the necessity of existence.

Core Idea
A single thing cannot be both preponderant and preponderated over simultaneously.

This lesson unpacks the third proof from al-Matalib concerning the impossibility of simultaneous preponderance of opposites. When a preponderating motive for existence is present, supposing non-existence also occurs leads to a contradiction. Understanding this argument is crucial for establishing that existence becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Let the logical structure sink in before deeper engagement.
Notice the conditional 'if... then...' structure.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The proof presents a dilemma: either non-existence becomes impossible (necessity follows) or it does not. In the second branch, a contradiction arises. Find where each branch is discussed.
3
Trace the contradiction in the second branch
Follow the reasoning: if non-existence is supposed to occur with the motive for existence, then both existence and non-existence would be preponderant in the same state. Understand why this is impossible.
The key phrase is 'كون وجوده راجحاً على عدمه، وعدمه راجحاً على وجوده'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن عند حصول الداعية المرجحة للوجود . إن امتنع العدم، فقد حصل الوجوب وهو المقصود . وإن لم يمتنع العدم ، فليفرض العدم حاصلاً مع حصول ذلك القدر من رجحان الوجود . لأن كل ما كان ممكناً ، لم يكن من فرض وقوعه محال . لكن هذا الفرض محال ، لأن حال حصول رجحان الوجود . لو حصل العدم يحصل في تلك الحالة رجحان العدم لأن عند حصول العدم، يكون العدم راجحاً ، لا محالة . وهذا يوجب أن يقال : إن عند حصول رجحان الوجود ، حصل رجحان العدم . وذلك يقتضي كون وجوده راجحاً على عدمه ، وعدمه راجحاً على وجوده ، باعتبار واحد ، في حال واحد وإنه محال
The third proof: When the preponderating motive for existence is obtained, if non-existence is impossible, then necessity (al-wujub) has occurred, and that is the purpose. And if non-existence is not impossible, then let non-existence be supposed as occurring while that degree of preponderance of existence is obtained. Because everything that is possible, its supposition is not impossible. But this supposition is impossible, because in the state of obtaining preponderance of existence, if non-existence occurred, then in that state there would be preponderance of non-existence, because when non-existence occurs, non-existence is preponderant, inevitably. This necessitates saying: When preponderance of existence is obtained, preponderance of non-existence is obtained. And that requires that its existence is preponderant over its non-existence, and its non-existence is preponderant over its existence, in one consideration, in one state, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the third proof. The author again presents a dilemma: when the preponderating motive (da'iya murajjiha) for existence is obtained, either non-existence is impossible (so necessity/wujub of existence follows) or it is not impossible. If not impossible, we suppose non-existence occurs with that same degree of preponderance for existence. But this supposition is impossible because if non-existence occurs in that state, then non-existence would also be preponderant (since when something occurs, that occurrence itself is preponderant). This would mean that at the same time, existence is preponderant (due to the motive) and non-existence is preponderant (due to actual occurrence). This is a contradiction: one thing cannot be both preponderant over its opposite and opposite over it in the same respect and state.

Key Terms
داعية مرجحة
وجوب
امتناع
رجحان وجود
رجحان عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can occur even when there is a motive for existence, without contradiction.
Correction: The proof shows that supposing non-existence occurs while the motive for existence is present leads to both existence and non-existence being preponderant simultaneously, which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof show when non-existence is supposed to occur with a preponderating motive for existence?
A) That existence becomes necessary.
B) That non-existence becomes impossible.
C) That both existence and non-existence become preponderant simultaneously.
D) That infinite regress follows.

Key Takeaway
The simultaneous preponderance of existence and non-existence is impossible because it would require contradictory states in the same respect and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that a preponderating motive leads to necessity, eliminating the possibility of its opposite. Can you identify any area in your life where you hold two contradictory inclinations? What would it mean to truly let one motive settle the matter decisively?
?
If you believe in a Creator who has a perfect motive for existence, does this proof challenge any lingering doubts about the necessity of His existence? How does accepting logical necessity change the way you approach faith?
?
When you consider the motives behind your actions, are you prepared to accept that a truly prevailing motive should make the opposite impossible? Reflect on a decision where you have been indecisive—what would it take for you to reach certainty?

The Preponderator Must Be Complete or Regress Follows
Argues that a preponderator must be complete or else infinite regress follows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a preponderator must be complete and necessitate its effect, otherwise an infinite regress or contradiction arises.

Core Idea
A partial cause leads to either no cause or endless regress.

This passage presents a rigorous argument from Islamic kalam about causality. It shows that if a preponderator (cause) is not fully sufficient to bring about its effect, then either the effect occurs without a cause, or we are forced into an infinite chain of additional factors. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to establish that the preponderator must be complete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated conditional structure.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The passage starts with a scenario: a certain amount of preponderance is present. Then it branches based on whether non-existence is impossible or not.
3
Trace the two sub-branches
When non-existence is possible, the same preponderance leads to existence at one time and non-existence at another. The argument examines whether the distinction depends on an extra qualification.
If no extra qualification, then the distinction occurs without any preponderator – absurd. If there is an extra qualification, then the original factor was not the complete preponderator – contradiction and regress.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since both alternatives lead to impossibility, the original assumption that the preponderator was not complete is false. Thus the preponderator must be complete and necessitate the effect.
This is a classic argument by contradiction (burhan al-tamanu').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لان حصول ذلك القدر من الرجحان . إن امتنع العدم، فقد حصل الوجود . وذلك هو المطلوب . وإن لم يمتنع العدم، فلنفرض ذلك واقعا . فإن كل ما كان ممكنا ، لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون قد حصل عند حصول ذلك القدر من الرجحان الوجود تارة والعدم أخرى بامتياز أحد الوقتين عن الآخر . بحصول الأثر في أحدهما ، وعدم حصوله في الثاني ، إن لم يتوقف على انضمام قيد إليه فقد يرجح الممكن المتساوي لا عن مرجح نسبة ذلك القدر من الرجحان إلى الوقتين، لما كان على السوية ، ولم يختص أحد الوقتين بمزية ، لأجله صار هو أولى بالوقوع ، فحينئذ يكون تمييز أحد الوقتين عن الثاني تمييزا لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي عن الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وإما أن يتوقف على انضمام قيد إليه ، فحينئذ يكون المرجح هو المجموع الحاصل من الأمر الذي كان حاصلا ، مع انضمام هذا القيد إليه فيلزم أن يقال : إن مجرد الأمر الذي كان حاصلا قبل ذلك ، ما كان تمام المرجح . مع انا فرضناه تمام المرجح . هذا خلف . وأيضا : يتحقق بقيد التقسيم عند حصول ذلك المجموع . فنقول : إن كان الفعل واجبا فهو المقصود . وإن لم يكن واجبا ، عاد التقسيم فيه . ولزم التسلسل . وهو محال
Because the occurrence of that amount of preponderance. If non-existence is impossible, then existence has occurred. And that is the desired. And if non-existence is not impossible, then let us suppose that as actual. For every possible thing, it is not necessary from supposing its occurrence to be absurd. And on this estimation, there would have occurred at the occurrence of that amount of preponderance existence sometimes and non-existence another time, by distinguishing one of the two times from the other. By the occurrence of the effect in one of them and its non-occurrence in the second, if it does not depend on an additional qualification, then the possible equal [thing] is given preponderance without a preponderator, because the proportion of that amount of preponderance to the two times, being equal, and one of the times does not have a special advantage, for which it would be more worthy of occurrence. So then the distinguishing of one time from the second is a distinguishing of one of the two sides of the possible equal from the other, without a preponderator. And if it depends on an additional qualification, then the preponderator is the total resulting from the thing that was present, with the addition of this qualification to it. So it follows that it be said: The mere thing that was present before that was not the complete preponderator, while we supposed it the complete preponderator. This is a contradiction. And also: It is verified by the qualification of division at the occurrence of that total. So we say: If the act is necessary, then it is the goal. And if it is not necessary, then the division recurs in it, and infinite regress follows. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents an argument concerning the completeness of the preponderator (المرجح) in causing an effect. It is a proof by contradiction and regress. The author begins with a scenario: a certain amount of preponderance (رجحان) is present. If non-existence is impossible, then existence necessarily occurs—that is the desired conclusion. If non-existence is not impossible, we suppose it can occur. Since the effect is possible, assuming its occurrence does not entail an absurdity. On this assumption, the same amount of preponderance would sometimes yield existence and sometimes non-existence, requiring a distinction between two otherwise identical times. If this distinction does not depend on an additional qualification (قيد), then the preponderator is equally related to both times, and no reason exists for one time to be preferred—so the differentiation occurs without any preponderator, which is absurd. If the distinction does depend on an additional qualification, then the true preponderator is the combination of the original factor and the new qualification. Hence the original factor alone was not the complete preponderator, contradicting the original claim. Moreover, if the combined preponderator still does not necessitate the effect, the same division applies, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). Thus the preponderator must be complete and necessitate the effect, or else an impossibility follows.

Key Terms
رجحان
مرجح
قيد
تسلسل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a partial cause can sometimes produce an effect without additional factors.
Correction: The argument shows that if a cause is partial, either the effect occurs without any cause (absurd) or the cause must be supplemented, leading to infinite regress unless the cause is complete.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens if the distinction between two times of effect occurrence depends on an additional qualification?
A) The original factor alone remains the complete preponderator.
B) The preponderator becomes the total of original factor plus qualification.
C) Infinite regress is avoided.
D) Non-existence becomes impossible.

Key Takeaway
A preponderator must be complete and necessitate its effect, otherwise either effect occurs without cause or infinite regress ensues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on partial causes—like your own effort, luck, or circumstances—as if they are sufficient, forgetting that only a complete cause (God) truly brings about effects? How can this argument remind you to trust in Allah alone as the complete preponderator?
?
When something good happens to you, do you sometimes attribute it to a secondary cause (e.g., your planning) without recognizing that the true preponderator must be complete and is ultimately Allah? Examine your heart: where do you place your trust?
?
If you believe that Allah's will is not fully effective in some area of your life (e.g., you doubt that He will provide), you are essentially assuming an incomplete preponderator. How does the logical necessity of a complete cause challenge you to strengthen your reliance on Allah?

The Third Argument: Simultaneous Preponderances are Impossible
Third argument: impossibility of simultaneous preponderance for both existence and non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument proves that a preponderating cause must make its effect necessary, otherwise contradictory preponderances would arise.

Core Idea
A preponderating cause cannot leave the effect possible without leading to a contradiction.

The third argument in this series uses a proof by contradiction. It assumes that a preponderating factor for existence is present, and then examines what happens if non-existence remains possible. The result is an absurdity: both existence and non-existence would be preponderant at the same time. This strengthens the case for causal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the conditional structure. Notice the branching into two cases.
The argument is compact; take your time to follow each step.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument splits at the presence of the preponderating factor: either non-existence becomes impossible (case one) or it does not (case two).
Case one leads directly to the desired conclusion (necessity).
3
Understand the contradiction
In case two, suppose non-existence occurs. Then both a preponderance for existence and a preponderance for non-existence would hold simultaneously—an impossibility.
This contradiction forces the conclusion that non-existence must be impossible when the preponderance is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن عند حصول الداعية المرجحة للوجود . إن امتنع العدم، فقد حصل الوجوب وهو المقصود . وإن لم يمتنع العدم ، فليفرض العدم حاصلا مع حصول ذلك القدر من رجحان الوجود . لأن كل ما كان ممكنا ، لم يكن من فرض وقوعه محال . لكن هذا الفرض محال ، لأن حال حصول رجحان الوجود . لو حصل العدم يحصل في تلك الحالة رجحان العدم لأن عند حصول العدم، يكون العدم راجحا ، لا محالة . وهذا يوجب أن يقال : إن عند حصول رجحان الوجود ، حصل رجحان العدم . وذلك يقتضي كون وجوده راجحا على عدمه ، وعدمه راجحا على وجوده ، باعتبار واحد ، في حال واحد وإنه محال
The third argument: When the motivating factor that gives preponderance to existence is present. If non-existence is impossible, then necessity has occurred, which is the goal. And if non-existence is not impossible, then let non-existence be supposed to occur with the occurrence of that amount of preponderance of existence. Because everything that is possible, it is not that its supposition of occurrence is absurd. But this supposition is absurd, because in the state of occurrence of the preponderance of existence, if non-existence occurred, there would occur in that state a preponderance of non-existence, because at the occurrence of non-existence, non-existence is preponderant necessarily. And this necessitates that it be said: At the occurrence of preponderance of existence, preponderance of non-existence occurred. And that requires its existence being preponderant over its non-existence, and its non-existence being preponderant over its existence, from one consideration, in one state, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the third argument (الحجة الثالثة) in a series. It is a proof by contradiction that the motivating preponderance (الداعية المرجحة) for existence must make non-existence impossible. The argument assumes the presence of a factor that preponderates existence. If non-existence is impossible, then existence is necessary—the desired conclusion. If non-existence is not impossible, we suppose it occurs together with that preponderance. Since the situation is possible, assuming non-existence is not inherently absurd. However, this supposition is absurd because at the very moment the preponderance for existence is present, if non-existence actually occurs, then non-existence must also be preponderant (since whatever occurs is preponderant). Thus we would have both preponderance for existence and preponderance for non-existence simultaneously for the same thing, from the same consideration. This is a logical contradiction, so the supposition is impossible. Therefore non-existence must be impossible when the preponderance for existence is present, establishing the necessity of the effect.

Key Terms
داعية
رجحان
وجوب
امتناع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a preponderating factor can exist without necessitating its effect.
Correction: The argument shows that if the factor does not necessitate, then both existence and non-existence would be preponderant simultaneously, which is a logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
What follows if we suppose non-existence occurs together with the preponderance for existence in the third argument?
A) Non-existence remains impossible.
B) Both preponderances for existence and non-existence would be present simultaneously.
C) The preponderance for existence is cancelled.
D) The act becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that a preponderating cause must make its effect necessary, otherwise contradictory preponderances would arise simultaneously.

Examine your heart: do you ever feel that a created cause (like a job interview) is what determines your outcome, rather than Allah's decree? How does this argument challenge that assumption? Commit today to remind yourself before every action that Allah is the only Necessitator.
?
If a preponderating cause necessitates its effect, then what does that imply about your reliance on means? Are you trusting in the means more than the Creator? This lesson forces you to see that all causes are subservient. Will you let this reshape your dua and your trust?
?
The argument shows that a preponderating cause cannot leave the effect possible without contradiction. How does this affect your view of personal responsibility? If Allah's will preponderates, then your actions are necessitated. Yet you feel choice. Can you live with this tension? What does that say about your need for divine guidance?

The Fourth Argument: Preponderance Must Necessitate the Act
Sanusi presents the fourth argument and answers an objection about the slave's preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth argument demonstrates that if the slave's preponderance does not necessitate the act, it leads to either chance or compulsion, both of which negate divine causality and the Maker.

Core Idea
The slave's preponderance must necessitate the act; otherwise, chance or compulsion follows.

This passage presents the fourth argument in a series defending causal efficacy. It examines a scenario where preponderance for existence exists yet non‑existence occurs. The argument shows that such a scenario forces one to admit either that the slave's choice is ineffective or that events happen by pure chance—both of which undermine rational theology. Read carefully to see how the author uses this dilemma to reject any notion of preponderance without necessitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the argument
The argument begins by supposing preponderance for existence exists but non-existence occurs. It then presents two possibilities: the preponderance is from another, or from none (chance). Mark these two branches.
Each leads to a problem: either the slave's preponderance is ineffective, or chance compels denial of the Maker.
3
Understand the objection and the response
The objection suggests that the slave's preponderance works only when unopposed. The response shows that even then, the preponderance must necessitate the effect; otherwise, the same preponderance could yield different outcomes, which is pure chance.
The response reinforces the necessity of causal efficacy.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice the conclusion: any alternative to necessitation leads to chance, which implies compulsion (jabr) and denies the Maker and causality. This solidifies the argument that the slave's preponderance must be effective.
This connects to the broader debate on free will and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنه إذا حصل ترجيح الوجود ، ثم فرضنا أن بقاء هذا الترجيح حصل العدم فحينئذ يكون ذلك العدم حاصلا ، لا بترجيحه ولا بإبقائه ، فإن كان ذلك الترجيح لمرجح آخر ، فيكون هذا اعترافا بأن ترجيح العبد لا أثر له في اقتضاء الرجحان ، وإنما المؤثر في الرجحان ترجيح غيره . فإن قلنا : بأن ذلك الترجيح وقع لا المرجح أصلا ، كان هذا قولا بأن ذلك العدم إنما ترجح لمحض الاتفاق من غير مرجح أصلا . إلا أن القول بالاتفاق يوجب القول بالجبر على ما بيناه غير مرة - ويوجب نفي الصانع ، ونفي جميع الآثار والمؤثرات . لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العبد إذا رجع جانب الوجود فهذا الترجيح إن بقي بحيث لا يعارض ترجيح مرجح ، وقع الفعل . وإن عارضه ترجيح مرجح آخر ، فحينئذ خرج عن مضاد العقل ؟ لأنا نقول : إذا بقي ذلك الترجيح ، خاليا عن العوارض . فإن وجب ترتب الأثر عليه ، فذاك هو المقصود . وإن لم يجب ترتب الأثر عليه ، فحينئذ أمكن أن يبقى ذلك القدر من الترجيح ، مع انه لا يقع الوجود بل يقع العدم فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون ترتب الوجود عليه تارة، وترتب العدم عليه أخرى ، لمحض الاتفاق . وذلك يوجب نفي التأثير والمؤثر . ويوجب القول أيضا بالجبر
The fourth argument: If preponderance of existence occurs, then we suppose that with the continuation of this preponderance non-existence occurs, then that non-existence would occur not by its own preponderance nor by its preservation. If that preponderance is for another preponderator, then this is an admission that the slave's preponderance has no effect in producing preponderance, and the only effective factor in preponderance is the preponderance of another. If we say that the preponderance occurred without any preponderator at all, this would be a saying that that non-existence was given preponderance solely by chance, without any preponderator at all. But the saying by chance necessitates the saying of compulsion, as we have explained more than once, and necessitates the denial of the Maker, and the denial of all effects and efficient causes. It is not said: Why is it not permissible to say that when the slave preponderates the side of existence, this preponderance, if it remains such that it is not opposed by the preponderance of another preponderator, the act occurs; and if it is opposed by the preponderance of another preponderator, then it leaves the opposite of reason? For we say: If that preponderance remains, free from countervailing factors, then if the effect necessarily follows upon it, that is the goal. And if the effect necessarily does not follow upon it, then it is possible that that amount of preponderance remains, while existence does not occur but rather non-existence occurs. Then it follows that the occurrence of existence upon it sometimes, and the occurrence of non-existence upon it another time, is by pure chance. And that necessitates the denial of influence and the influential, and necessitates also the saying of compulsion.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument (الحجة الرابعة). It addresses a scenario where preponderance for existence is present, but we suppose non-existence actually occurs despite the continuation of that preponderance. In that case, non-existence would occur without its own preponderance or preservation. If the preponderance that occurs is from another preponderator, then the slave's preponderance is ineffective. If we say the preponderance occurs without any preponderator at all, then non-existence occurs by pure chance (اتفاق). But chance leads to compulsion (جبر) because it denies the influence of the agent, and it also denies the existence of the Maker and all causes and effects. Then the author presents an objection (لا يقال): why not say that when the slave gives preponderance to existence, and that preponderance remains without being opposed by another preponderator, the act follows; but if it is opposed, then reason rejects it? The answer (أنا نقول): if the preponderance remains free of countervailing factors, then either the effect necessarily follows—which is the goal—or it does not necessarily follow. If it does not necessarily follow, then the same preponderance can sometimes produce existence and sometimes non-existence, which is pure chance. That again negates causality and leads to compulsion. Thus the slave's preponderance must necessitate the act if no hindrance exists; otherwise, any alternative results in chance or the denial of efficacy.

Key Terms

مرجح
اتفاق

تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A preponderance can be fully present yet not guarantee the effect; the choice remains effective even if the act does not follow.
Correction: The argument shows that if the same preponderance sometimes yields existence and sometimes non‑existence, the outcome is determined by pure chance, not by the agent's choice. This leads to compulsion (jabr) because the agent's preponderance has no causal influence.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude about the consequence of saying that non-existence occurs by chance?
A) It affirms the slave's free will.
B) It necessitates the denial of the Maker.
C) It proves the preponderator is incomplete.
D) It allows for multiple preponderators.

Key Takeaway
The slave's preponderance must be sufficient to necessitate the act; otherwise, outcomes reduce to pure chance or external compulsion, both of which deny causal efficacy and the Maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this argument challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices before Allah? What step will you take today to ensure your preponderance is sincere and effective?
?
The argument shows that chance leads to compulsion. In your life, do you sometimes attribute good or bad to 'luck' or 'fate' in a way that denies your own causal role? What would it mean for you to accept that your preponderance must necessitate your acts, and how does that change your view of accountability?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt your intention (preponderance) did not lead to the expected action. According to this argument, that cannot happen if your preponderance is genuine. What does this reveal about the strength of your resolve or the sincerity of your intentions? What commitment will you make to align your inner preponderance with your actions for Allah's sake?

Fifth Argument: Will Necessitates Action When No Hindrance
Fifth argument: all rational people know by necessity that will leads to action when no hindrance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all rational people know by necessity that a capable agent must act when he wills and there is no hindrance.

Core Idea
Will plus no hindrance equals necessary action, as all rational people know.

This lesson presents a fifth argument drawn from necessary knowledge. The author begins with a concise maxim and then unpacks its meaning, appealing to universal rational intuition. You will see how the argument uses the example of will and abandonment to establish a necessary connection between volition and action when obstacles are absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening maxim
Start by reading 'الترك ترك' and think about what it might mean. The commentary suggests it means abandonment is a positive act of will, not mere absence.
This maxim is the foundation of the argument.
2
Identify the necessary knowledge claim
Identify the claim that all rational people know by necessity that if a capable agent wills an act and there is no hindrance, he must do it. Similarly for abandonment.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Observe the counterexample
Note that the author says no rational person would claim the opposite: that they will to do but do not do, or will not to do but do. This strengthens the claim.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that this is a decisive proof for the necessary connection between will and action in the absence of hindrance. The ending is cut off, but the main point is clear.
The argument may continue on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الترك ترك. وهذا يفيد أن العلم الضروري حاصل ، بأن القادر إذا شاء الفعل ، ولم يكن هناك مانع ، فإنه لابد وأن يفعل . وإذا شاء الترك ولم يكن هناك مانع ، فإنه لا بد وأن يترك . ولا نرى في الدنيا عاقلا يقول : إني إن شئت أن أفعل لم أفعل . وإن شئت أن لا أفعل فعلت . فثبت : أن هذا دليل قاطع على أن جميع العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة : أن القادر متى أراد الفعل ، ولا مانع له عنه ، فإنه يفعله لا محالة . وإذا أراد الترك ، ولا مانع له عن الترك ، فإنه يترك . وثبت : أن القول بحصول الفعل عند إرادة الشرك،
The fifth argument: Abandonment is abandonment. And this conveys that necessary knowledge is obtained: that when the capable agent wills the act, and there is no hindrance, then he must do it. And when he wills abandonment, and there is no hindrance, then he must abandon. And we do not see in the world a rational person saying: 'If I will to do, I do not do; and if I will not to do, I do.' So it is established that this is a decisive proof that all rational people know by necessity: that when the capable agent intends the act, and there is no hindrance for him from it, then he does it inevitably. And when he intends abandonment, and there is no hindrance for him from abandonment, then he abandons. And it is established: that the saying that the act occurs at the will of shirk...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is the fifth argument (الحجة الخامسة). It begins with a maxim 'الترك ترك' meaning 'abandonment is abandonment'—perhaps indicating that abandonment is a positive act of will, not mere absence. The argument appeals to necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري): all rational people instinctively know that if a capable agent (القادر) wills an act and there is no hindrance (مانع), he must perform it. Similarly, if he wills to abandon and there is no hindrance, he must abandon. No rational person would claim the opposite. Therefore, this is a decisive proof that necessity is inherent in the relationship between will and action when no obstacle exists. The argument is cut off mid-sentence, but it appears to be concluding that the claim that the act occurs when willing shirk (إرادة الشرك) leads to a contradiction or further conclusion. The incomplete sentence suggests the text continues on a missing page.

Correction: The argument clarifies that the absence of hindrance is a necessary condition for the necessity of action. Will without hindrance leads to necessary action; if a hindrance exists, the outcome is not determined by will alone.
